201504	1405	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Williams.	13	1
201504	1406	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201504	1407	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	As many have already noted, the recent data have been disappointing, but it is worth noting, as President Lockhart just did, that over the past four years, first-quarter GDP growth has averaged more than 2 percentage points lower than growth during the rest of the year, and there is no single smoking gun that can explain this string of seasonal aberrations.	13	1
201504	1408	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Each year, the winter slowdowns have proved to be temporary.	13	1
201504	1409	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	So, if past is prologue, I expect the same pattern will occur this year, with an anemic Q1 followed by above-trend growth for the rest of 2015.	13	1
201504	1410	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’m confident that the underlying momentum in the economy once again will be more apparent as the first quarter fades in our rearview mirror.	13	1
201504	1411	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This is consistent with what I’ve been hearing from my business contacts.	13	1
201504	1412	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In particular, the fundamentals of the economy remain quite positive.	13	1
201504	1413	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Solid job growth should lift income and consumer confidence further, helping to pave the way for continued consumption growth.	13	1
201504	1414	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In addition, highly accommodative monetary policy will continue to boost spending, and these factors are very evident in my District.	13	1
201504	1415	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In fact, some sectors of the economy, the 12th District economy, are red hot.	13	1
201504	1416	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	An executive in the tech sector recently mentioned that to retain talent, some tech firms are now offering 20 percent or more pay raises.	13	1
201504	1417	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And if that wasn’t enough, they’re also throwing in housing for good measure, and in the Bay Area, housing is something that is very hard to come by and obviously very dear.	13	1
201504	1418	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The other thing that we heard was that you can be vested in stock options after one month.	13	1
201504	1419	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	So this is an amazing time in the Silicon Valley economy, and about half of my staff ran off to apply for jobs.	13	1
201504	1420	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In addition, I will mention that some of my contacts in different sectors, including commercial real estate, have emphasized the abundance of financing that can be obtained at very generous terms.	13	1
201504	1421	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s an issue that I noticed was highlighted in both the SLOOS and the QS reports, and we’re hearing about that in our District, too.	13	1
201504	1422	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Of course, continued low inflation remains a key issue.	13	1
201504	1423	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I view the recent low inflation readings as being consistent with an economy still operating below potential and facing temporary disinflationary factors.	13	1
201504	1424	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Important questions remain.	13	1
201504	1425	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In the remainder of my remarks, I’ll focus on two of these issues.	13	1
201504	1426	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The first is, what signal should we be taking from the market-implied measures of inflation compensation?	13	1
201504	1427	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And, second, what’s the empirical relationship between wage growth and inflation?	13	1
201504	1428	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The softness in inflation of late would be a lot more troublesome to me if it was also accompanied by a clear decline in inflation expectations.	13	1
201504	1429	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This kind of scenario is discussed in the Tealbook alternative scenario simulation.	13	1
201504	1430	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	But so far, the weight of evidence suggests that this isn’t happening.	13	1
201504	1431	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Long-run survey expectations from households and professional forecasters remain well anchored.	13	1
201504	1432	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In addition, as I pointed out in the past, long-run inflation expectations implied by a Phillips curve model estimated over the past 15 years, a period during which our credibility was firmly established, were also well anchored at 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1433	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, of course, the one discordant note comes from the market-implied measures of inflation compensation.	13	1
201504	1434	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Inflation compensation has fallen significantly since the middle of last year, and while much of the focus has been on longer-horizon measures, there’s also a question about what signal we should take about the nearer-term inflation outlook.	13	1
201504	1435	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	My staff looked at this question, and they conducted a forecasting comparison between measures of inflation compensation and five other competing forecasts, among them a random walk forecast and also the survey from the Survey of Professional Forecasters.	13	1
201504	1436	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We wanted to avoid liquidity issues that are associated with shorter-term TIPS, so they looked at inflation compensation derived from inflation swap rates.	13	1
201504	1437	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	These typically move very closely together with inflation compensation derived from the Treasury market, but are based on trading in a deeper market.	13	1
201504	1438	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Here we’re looking at one- and two-year-ahead inflation forecasts for headline CPI inflation.	13	1
201504	1439	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The striking result, which is very robust in all of their experiments, is that forecasts based on inflation compensation were the worst performers of all the measures.	13	1
201504	1440	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Basically, inflation compensation did not give useful information about where inflation was going relative to survey forecasts.	13	1
201504	1441	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	So, really, looking at inflation compensation for insights into the future path of inflation is not supported by the data.	13	1
201504	1442	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	One problem with our analysis is that data limitations preclude doing a similar exercise for long-term inflation expectations.	13	1
201504	1443	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’m going to go out on a limb here and say that the results we have for one- and two-year inflation compensation not being a useful predictor for future inflation will apply also to longer-term inflation.	13	1
201504	1444	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We don’t have that much data right now, but in five years when the transcript comes out, we’ll see whether I was right or wrong.	13	1
201504	1445	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We’ll wait for that.	13	1
201504	1446	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Again, President Kocherlakota has highlighted the fact—in a memo that he co- authored—that inflation compensation is not a reliable forecast for inflation because it reflects other aspects of risk associated with different inflation outcomes.	13	1
201504	1447	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	But I want to mention another interesting finding in our work about inflation compensation, and that is that these one- and two- year-ahead inflation compensation measures are very highly correlated with recent past inflation, especially with past inflation associated with movements in oil prices.	13	1
201504	1448	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	That puts into question how to interpret this.	13	1
201504	1449	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It is a very similar result that we’ve found in the past when we looked at household surveys.	13	1
201504	1450	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Household surveys of inflation expectations tend to be very sensitive to lagged inflation, especially with regard to oil price movements, and for this reason we have tended to discount movements in household inflation expectations when the movements are driven by energy prices.	13	1
201504	1451	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	My staff suggests using a similar caution when trying to interpret inflation compensation measures as an indicator of people’s views on future inflation.	13	1
201504	1452	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The second issue I’ll discuss briefly is that of nominal wage growth.	13	1
201504	1453	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	As has already been mentioned, wage growth has been stuck around 2 percent for most of the recovery.	13	1
201504	1454	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And, as economic theory would tell us, employee compensation makes up a huge fraction of a firm’s costs.	13	1
201504	1455	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	You would expect there to be a pretty tight relationship between nominal wage growth and price inflation, but this connection has been remarkably hard to find.	13	1
201504	1456	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Many papers and research projects have looked at this, and, basically, if you look back at the history of the literature, in every business cycle there’s a whole bunch of papers written about the relationship of nominal wage growth and price inflation.	13	1
201504	1457	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The consistent finding through this literature is that it’s really hard to find any relationship between nominal wage growth and future price inflation.	13	1
201504	1458	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Recent research by Jim Stock and Mark Watson, and also some really nice work done at the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland and elsewhere, has shown that the additional information on wages or labor compensation has done little to improve the accuracy of standard inflation forecast models.	13	1
201504	1459	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	My understanding is that the staff’s inflation forecasting models tend to be these price‒price Phillips curve models or models that don’t really emphasize nominal wage growth.	13	1
201504	1460	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The lack of a close empirical link between nominal wage growth and future inflation likely reflects several factors that have affected labor markets in recent decades—some of which I’ve talked about in the past.	13	1
201504	1461	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	One is that nominal wage growth is probably being held down by rigidities in wage setting that kept wages from falling during the downturn and have been restraining wage growth as the economy improves—that is, there is downward nominal wage rigidity.	13	1
201504	1462	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The second is that globalization, changes in unionization, and changes in technology have affected the share of productivity gains that are going to workers, and that distorts the relationship between wage growth and price inflation.	13	1
201504	1463	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Put another way, the labor share of income has fallen dramatically since the 1980s.	13	1
201504	1464	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	To summarize, based on the evidence accumulated over the past several decades of extensive research on this topic, there’s little or no information to suggest that aggregate wage data should inform our medium-term inflation outlook.	13	1
201504	1465	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now I’m going to break my rule that I stated earlier—I do not view a rise in nominal wage growth as a necessary condition to be confident in the return of inflation to 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1466	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	All told, our current low inflation rates, I think, are pretty easy to understand in the context of transitory factors—import prices, energy prices— and the fact that there is still some considerable slack in the labor market.	13	1
201504	1467	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	As the labor market moves back to maximum employment, and as these transitory factors dissipate, I expect inflation to move back to 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1468	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	President Bullard mentioned 2011.	13	1
201504	1469	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think we should learn a lesson from that.	13	1
201504	1470	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We did see inflation rise quite a bit as a result of a rise in some special factors.	13	1
201504	1471	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We were experiencing high inflationary pressures due to large increases in the price of oil and other import prices, and what we did then, I think, is the right thing.	13	1
201504	1472	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We looked past the transitory effects of those and looked more broadly at the long-term inflation outlook, and that is how I am viewing it, too.	13	1

201504	1509	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Okay.	13	1
201504	1510	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201504	1511	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	For the most part, my directors and business contacts expect that the recent spate of soft economic readings will be short-lived.	13	1
201504	1512	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	The one report that seems to sum it up the best came from the chairman of Manpower who, when talking about soft first-quarter numbers, said none of his clients are getting depressed about it.	13	1
201504	1513	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	The view seems to be that there is enough momentum from last year that growth should pick up soon.	13	1
201504	1514	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Separately, I didn’t hear of any meaningful reports of wage or price pressures from my contacts.	13	1
201504	1515	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	My own outlook for the real economy continues to be for real GDP growth to run between 2½ and 3 percent over the remainder of this year and next.	13	1
201504	1516	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Our assumptions for potential output growth are somewhat stronger than in the Tealbook, so our GDP path translates into a very similar reduction in resource gaps as the Tealbook’s.	13	1
201504	1517	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	By the end of 2016, we have the unemployment rate down to around 5 percent, which is my current assessment of the natural rate.	13	1
201504	1518	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	My inflation forecast has not changed.	13	1
201504	1519	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Like the Tealbook, I do not see inflation reaching target until sometime in 2018 or 2019.	13	1
201504	1520	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I should again point out that my assessment of appropriate monetary policy underlying this forecast has lower rates than most people around the table.	13	1
201504	1521	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Now, I want to spend some time today talking about why this outlook leads me to favor a whites-of-their-eyes approach for our policy response to rising inflation.	13	1
201504	1522	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I have talked about risk management many times before.	13	1
201504	1523	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Today I want to articulate three additional reasons the data lead me to prefer a cautious approach to raising rates.	13	1
201504	1524	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	First, forecasting inflation is inherently difficult, and that’s the discussion that we were just having.	13	1
201504	1525	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	This implies a high burden of proof to say we are sufficiently confident that our currently low core inflation will move back to 2 percent in a reasonable period of time.	13	1
201504	1526	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Second, despite near-zero nominal rates and a large balance sheet, I am not convinced our current policy stance is all that accommodative to begin with.	13	1
201504	1527	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	And, third, I actually see benefits to risking a modest overshooting of our 2 percent inflation target.	13	1
201504	1528	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Let me start with why it should take strong evidence to have confidence in an outlook of low inflation rising back to 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1529	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Anyone who has looked at the forecasting performance of statistical inflation models knows that everyone’s abilities in this area are limited.	13	1
201504	1530	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Indeed, there is a body of research that shows it is not that easy to be a random walk forecast.	13	1
201504	1531	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Even the best- performing models seldom forecast a rapid change in inflation, and they always imply substantial forecast uncertainty.	13	1
201504	1532	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	That said, we do have many analytical reasons to think our current low inflation will rise at some point.	13	1
201504	1533	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Most of the commentary I hear at this table centers on the economy regaining traction, resource gaps closing, and the upward gravitational pull of high and stable inflation expectations, like in the Tealbook forecast.	13	1
201504	1534	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I also suspect that simple mean reversion probably plays a role in these forecasts as well.	13	1
201504	1535	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I agree that these forces will eventually deliver.	13	1
201504	1536	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	The question is, when?	13	1
201504	1537	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Taking until 2019 to reach our target is not acceptable to me.	13	1
201504	1538	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Given all of these difficulties in forecasting inflation, my own preferred dashboard of indicators is mostly a list of factors coincident with rising inflation.	13	1
201504	1539	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	While measures of labor market slack are important, I would not rely on them alone, as it is possible something has changed in the structure of the economy that makes those historical benchmarks less trustworthy.	13	1
201504	1540	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	In addition, I will continue to look for increasing core inflation, faster wage growth, and firmer market-based measures of inflation compensation to provide corroborating evidence of stronger inflationary pressures.	13	1
201504	1541	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	As I said, the absence of statistically strong leading indicators suggests that these observations are likely to be more coincident than predictive.	13	1
201504	1542	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	But at least once we see them, we can have more confidence that inflation is moving up persistently.	13	1
201504	1543	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Until then, I remain wary.	13	1
201504	1544	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Now, my second point—that current policy is not as accommodative as we might think— comes out of the model analysis.	13	1
201504	1545	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	A fundamental benchmark for thinking about the level of monetary accommodation and inflation pressures is the difference between the actual real rate of interest and the equilibrium, or Wicksellian, real rate of interest.	13	1
201504	1546	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	As Milton Friedman said, low interest rates alone do not mean monetary policy is accommodative.	13	1
201504	1547	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	The relevant question is whether, after adjusting for inflation, our current real federal funds rate is far enough below the equilibrium real rate to achieve our policy objectives in a reasonable amount of time—that is, for inflation to actually get up to 2 percent, as the forecasts embody.	13	1
201504	1548	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I think the answer to that question is “no.”	13	1
201504	1549	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	There are a number of simple observations that suggest that the current equilibrium rate is quite low.	13	1
201504	1550	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Namely, even with our interest rate and balance sheet policies in place, we still see the continued existence of resource gaps, a lack of any meaningful upward movement in inflation, and relatively low levels of capital spending despite piles of cash on corporate balance sheets.	13	1
201504	1551	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	These cash stockpiles are an indication that expected real returns on physical investment are not very high.	13	1
201504	1552	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	To put a number out, the Tealbook-consistent equilibrium real rate shown in Book B, as circulated over the weekend, is negative 0.1 percent.	13	1
201504	1553	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Some might look at the current real funds rate of minus 1.18 percent, as reported in the Tealbook, Book B, and say we have adequate accommodation in place.	13	1
201504	1554	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I don’t agree.	13	1
201504	1555	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Recall that the Tealbook’s equilibrium real rate is calibrated to close the output gap in 12 quarters.	13	1
201504	1556	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	It says nothing about inflation.	13	1
201504	1557	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	In the Tealbook baseline, the inflation gap is still with us until 2019.	13	1
201504	1558	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	That would mean that inflation would have to run below target for more than a decade and, to me, that is simply too long to be considered the result of appropriate policy.	13	1
201504	1559	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I want to mention that President Bullard referred to the fact that PCE inflation rose to 2 percent in 2011 and 2012 on the strength of commodity price increases.	13	1
201504	1560	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I think the reason we focus on core inflation is that core is a better indicator of what inflation is likely to be next year, because the shocks in headline PCE often are transitory.	13	1
201504	1561	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	The real test, I would say, is, when did the Tealbook ever forecast inflation two years out as being at 2 percent or above?	13	1
201504	1562	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I think that has not been the case for quite some time.	13	1
201504	1563	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	While we can all lament the fact that there are large uncertainties regarding the value of the equilibrium real rate of interest, uncertainty over inflation forecasting and the stance of monetary policy accommodation go hand in hand.	13	1
201504	1564	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Logically, because the equilibrium rate has a strong bearing on the inflation outlook, uncertainty over it ought to translate into comparable uncertainty over inflation itself.	13	1
201504	1565	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	In other words, how can we declare we are confident that current low inflation is headed back up to 2 percent unless we are also confident that our policy setting relative to the equilibrium real rate is able to return inflation to target?	13	1
201504	1566	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	This brings me to the third reason my outlook is premised on a whites-of-their-eyes policy approach.	13	1
201504	1567	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I recognize that the usual policy lags might mean that a delay in tightening could cause inflation to modestly overshoot 2 percent sometime down the road.	13	1
201504	1568	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	But so what?	13	1
201504	1569	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Is that such a big cost?	13	1
201504	1570	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	No.	13	1
201504	1571	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	On the contrary, it could be a benefit.	13	1
201504	1572	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	Considering how long inflation has been below target, adopting a policy that allows the possibility of such an overshoot actually seems necessary to support the credibility of our symmetric inflation objective.	13	1
201504	1573	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	If we find ourselves with the federal funds rate at 1 percent or a little bit above that while inflation is still too low, we could easily regret the conservative credibility that we’ve built up.	13	1
201504	1574	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I would also say that if we are not willing to allow an overshoot of our inflation objective, then I think we should definitely be having the conversation that President Rosengren was suggesting we have in terms of the appropriateness of the level of the inflation objective, if it is something that we are not allowed to overshoot with any reasonability.	13	1
201504	1575	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I think that’s important.	13	1

201504	1631	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Data on economic conditions in the Eighth District have improved notably during the intermeeting period.	13	1
201504	1632	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The pace of job creation was revised up for 2014, and preliminary data for 2015 has generally been positive.	13	1
201504	1633	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The District unemployment rate continues to decline and stands at 5.7 percent today, according to the most recent reading—only slightly higher than the national rate.	13	1
201504	1634	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	According to the revised BLS data, District job creation occurred at about twice the pace in 2014 as compared with 2013, which was itself revised higher.	13	1
201504	1635	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	One of the largest revisions was in the Louisville MSA which was revised from an original estimated value of 2.3 percent growth to an updated value of 3.8 percent.	13	1
201504	1636	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	By sector, notable upward revisions occurred in transportation, manufacturing, and professional and business services.	13	1
201504	1637	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Correspondingly, Louisville’s average hourly earnings growth is significantly faster than the national average.	13	1
201504	1638	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	On the downside, one of the most notable developments in the local labor market was the announcement by US Steel that they would temporarily idle their Granite City, Illinois, plant, laying off over 2,000 employees effective in May.	13	1
201504	1639	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Media reports suggest that the layoffs are due, in part, to a rise in imports of relatively inexpensive Chinese steel—steel that, evidently, the Chinese do not need in their home market.	13	1
201504	1640	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	On this dimension, my recent intermeeting visit to China, combined with discussions with CEOs and my interpretation of some hard data, has suggested to me that trend growth in China may be notably slower than commonly appreciated.	13	1
201504	1641	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think this goes beyond the relatively weak reported growth rate in the first quarter to a trend rate of growth as low as 4½ percent.	13	1
201504	1642	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Chinese GDP statistics are notably opaque—a topic of macroeconomic discussion for the past 15 years or more—but corroborating data and anecdotes now seem to be diverging more sharply from official reports, suggesting that a Chinese slowdown is to be taken far more seriously this time.	13	1
201504	1643	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The proximate cause of the slowdown is likely the ongoing anticorruption campaign, which is reportedly casting a very wide net and is likely creating a chilling effect on new business formation and business expansion.	13	1
201504	1644	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Turning to the national outlook, I have five main areas for comments on the current narrative for the United States, as reported in the Tealbook, FOMC speeches, and popular commentary.	13	1
201504	1645	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The comments are in the following areas:  one, potential over-interpretation of the Q1 slowdown; two, an unemployment forecast that I think is not credible; three, assumptions about r* that may be suspect; four, over-interpretation of the likely effects of the value of the dollar on US growth prospects; and, five, underappreciation of the risks of asset price bubbles during the next two to three years as rates remain exceptionally low.	13	1
201504	1646	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m going to comment on the first three of these today.	13	1
201504	1647	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m not commenting directly on inflation because I think the situation in that dimension has not changed greatly during the intermeeting period.	13	1
201504	1648	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I continue to expect inflation to return to target and eventually overshoot 2 percent in 2016.	13	1
201504	1649	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	On the first issue, potential over-interpretation of the Q1 slowdown, data are clearly weaker on a tracking basis since our previous meeting.	13	1
201504	1650	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	GDP will be reported tomorrow morning, and the staff has penciled in an estimate close to zero.	13	1
201504	1651	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	But suppose we take the median of the nowcasting table reported in the Tealbook, which is an approximately 1½ percent annual growth rate of GDP in the first quarter.	13	1
201504	1652	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	On that basis, the year-over-year growth rate would be 3.3 percent.	13	1
201504	1653	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think this number gives a better sense of the underlying pace of growth in the US economy and provides a better point of reference for shaping GDP expectations for the remainder of this year.	13	1
201504	1654	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	In addition, I think that seasonal factors for Q1 are being called into question.	13	1
201504	1655	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We considered Q1 GDP growth averages versus averages for Q2 through Q4 over different time periods, excluding recessions, since the mid-1990s.	13	1
201504	1656	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Average Q1 real GDP growth has been notably lower in these subperiods.	13	1
201504	1657	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This broad-brush analysis suggests that low Q1 growth rates are not occurring by accident, and that Q1 seasonals may need revamping.	13	1
201504	1658	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The second issue is unemployment projections balancing out at about 5 percent over the forecast horizon despite easy monetary policy, by conventional definition.	13	1
201504	1659	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I do not think that such a forecast is credible, and I continue to disagree with the labor force participation assumption behind it.	13	1
201504	1660	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	A key paper in the Brookings Papers on Economic Activity—by Aaronson and others, published in 2014—suggests that labor force participation has been dropping according to demographic factors since 2000, and, furthermore, that this downward trend will continue through the forecast horizon.	13	1
201504	1661	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This suggests that, on balance, there will be no sustained upward improvement in labor force participation and, therefore, that unemployment will continue to drop into the 4½ to 4 percent range over the forecast horizon, barring any major disruption in the US economy.	13	1
201504	1662	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This dynamic would be very similar to what was observed during the 1990s expansion and again during the 2000 expansion.	13	1
201504	1663	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	On both of those occasions, unemployment moved into the low 4 percent range.	13	1
201504	1664	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Previous staff predictions in this area have emphasized increases in labor force participation but have turned out to be wrong.	13	1
201504	1665	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Indeed, the predictions given in the Summary of Economic Projections have also relied, in part, on this story and have also been wrong.	13	1
201504	1666	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The median SEP forecast as of September 2012 for unemployment at the end of 2014 was too high by more than 1 percentage point.	13	1
201504	1667	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The same was true of the median SEP September 2013 forecast for unemployment.	13	1
201504	1668	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	So what’s the bottom line?	13	1
201504	1669	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’re charting a course for very low interest rate monetary policy based on an unrealistic assessment of future labor market performance, and this will harm FOMC policymaking in the quarters and years ahead.	13	1
201504	1670	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We can, of course, cite other labor market indicators and thereby bring to bear more information on overall labor market performance.	13	1
201504	1671	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The Board of Governors’ labor market conditions index does exactly this, conveniently summarizing information on many different dimensions of labor market performance.	13	1
201504	1672	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The Federal Reserve Bank of St Louis calculated that the value of this index is comfortably above its long-run average since 1986.	13	1
201504	1673	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This indicates that, based on the totality of available information, labor markets are not weak but instead are relatively strong viewed in historical perspective.	13	1
201504	1674	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I like this index because it brings all information together in one place and avoids cherry-picking particular indicators that may or may not be indicative of overall labor market performance.	13	1
201504	1675	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The third issue is r*.	13	1
201504	1676	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Some commentary has suggested that an r* parameter in a Taylor rule is low based on estimates of Laubach and Williams, which, combined with relatively small inflation and unemployment gaps, would suggest, through a Taylor-rule-type calculation, that current policy is approximately the policy recommended by the rule.	13	1
201504	1677	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This argument could rationalize current policy settings, although it would suggest that policy is perhaps not as accommodative as people would intuitively think.	13	1
201504	1678	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	One comment is that the value of r* is highly uncertain, either from a theoretical perspective or a measurement perspective.	13	1
201504	1679	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Time-varying r* Taylor rules are relatively untested and do not represent the empirical tradition behind Taylor rule estimation and recommended policy outcomes.	13	1
201504	1680	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	For this reason, I am skeptical of using time-varying r* as a rationale for current policy settings.	13	1
201504	1681	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The value of r* from a broader perspective should represent the Wicksellian natural rate of interest, which one might view as governed by the real rate of interest that would prevail in an economy without frictions and without monetary policy influence.	13	1
201504	1682	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	In a wide class of models, we know what the real rate of interest would be.	13	1
201504	1683	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	It would be the sum of the rate of productivity growth and the rate of labor force growth.	13	1
201504	1684	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	These factors alone would drive the real output growth rate in this class of models.	13	1
201504	1685	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	My staff calculated an r* value based on such a view.	13	1
201504	1686	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	It turned out to be 2 percent today, and this seems like a good benchmark for r* from which to calibrate Taylor rule policy recommendations.	13	1
201504	1687	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Such recommendations would suggest that policy is indeed accommodative today, which would match most people’s intuition about current policy settings.	13	1
201504	1688	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Interestingly, this value of r* has historically been somewhat lower than the Laubach- Williams measure.	13	1
201504	1689	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	It has been near zero in recent years, but as of today has moved above zero to a value of about 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1690	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The bottom line is that there are many ways to look at this value of r*, but a sensible guess is that r* is 2 percent or higher, meaning policy today is accommodative, as intuition suggests.	13	1
201504	1691	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201504	1692	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201504	1693	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Madam Chair, if I could—President Bullard, I was a little bit confused by what you were saying about labor force participation, and particularly the staff’s assessment of it.	13	1
201504	1694	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	My understanding of what the staff has been saying is that while they project a downward trend in labor force participation because of demographics, they also assess the decline in the immediate post-crisis years to have been substantially steeper than that.	13	1
201504	1695	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And what they were predicting—and continue, I think, to predict, unless Bill corrects me—is a less steep decline or a flattening-out of current labor force participation, which eventually has their projected demographic trend–based participation rate converging with the actual participation rate.	13	1
201504	1696	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And that, I think, is what’s been happening over the past year and a half or so, during which, with a little bit of fluctuation up and down, the labor force participation rate has been roughly stable, which I think is bearing out the staff’s expectation of the convergence of their projected trend with actual labor force participation.	13	1
201504	1697	2015-04-29	meeting	WASCHER	Yes, I think that’s how I interpret it as well.	13	1
201504	1698	2015-04-29	meeting	WASCHER	I don’t think what we have in the forecast is at all at odds with the Aaronson and others paper of 2014.	13	1
201504	1699	2015-04-29	meeting	WASCHER	The gap we have in there is roughly what we estimated in that paper, and as Governor Tarullo indicated, we expect the downtrend in the participation rate to continue and the actual participation rate to close the gap by falling less than the downtrend we have.	13	1
201504	1700	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	First Vice President Prichard.	13	1
201504	1701	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201504	1702	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Economic activity in the Third District slowed during the first quarter.	13	1
201504	1703	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	While still expanding, the rate of expansion has been below expectations.	13	1
201504	1704	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	However, it appears that the recent slowdown may be temporary, and that sentiment is widely held among contacts in our region.	13	1
201504	1705	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Unemployment in the region ticked up to 5.7 percent in March, with Pennsylvania experiencing a significant decline in employment, enough that the first-quarter job growth in the state was actually negative.	13	1
201504	1706	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	However, both our April Manufacturing Business Outlook Survey and Nonmanufacturing Business Outlook Survey indicate that the manufacturing and service sectors expanded employment in April and point to further job growth and capital expenditures over the second half of the year.	13	1

201504	1708	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Although he doesn’t anticipate 2015 to be as strong as last year, the second half of the year should see solid growth.	13	1
201504	1709	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	In response to a special survey question on the effect of the dollar’s appreciation on their manufacturing activity, respondents who have exchange rate exposure indicated that the appreciation negatively affected their operations.	13	1
201504	1710	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	However, our District does not overly engage in foreign trade, with exports accounting for only 10 percent of revenues and imports representing about 5 percent of nonlabor costs.	13	1
201504	1711	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Thus, the overall effect of the dollar’s rise was fairly small for Third District manufacturers.	13	1
201504	1712	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Both nonresidential and residential construction weakened in March.	13	1

201504	1714	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Reflecting the overall weaknesses in this sector, house price appreciation is also slower than in the nation as a whole, with prices growing in the 3 to 3½ percent range.	13	1
201504	1715	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Retailers in the region have been reporting healthy sales over the intermeeting period and moderate year-over-year sales growth.	13	1
201504	1716	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Contacts report that sales are starting to be bolstered by lower gasoline prices, and consumers in the region remain highly confident.	13	1
201504	1717	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	They are also paying down post-holiday debt, and debit and credit card lines are declining.	13	1
201504	1718	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Bankers in the region indicate that the growth in the commercial loan segment of their portfolios has picked up substantially, while other loan activity has remained fairly flat.	13	1
201504	1719	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Thus, our District reflects fairly well what might be transpiring in other regions nationally—a bit of a step back in labor markets, a falloff in manufacturing activity, and a lack of any significant improvement in residential investment.	13	1
201504	1720	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	This slowdown is well reflected in one of the series we produce in our real-time research center, the Aruoba-Diebold-Scotti Business Conditions Index.	13	1
201504	1721	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	The ADS uses six high-frequency data series to construct an index of current economic activity.	13	1
201504	1722	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Those series include, one, initial jobless claims through the week ending April 11 of this year; two, payroll employment through March; three, industrial production through March; four, real personal income through February; five, real manufacturing and trade sales through January; and, six, real GDP through the fourth quarter of last year.	13	1
201504	1723	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	The index is centered on zero, which indicates normal growth rates, and the index has recently turned negative.	13	1
201504	1724	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	However, we are receiving indications that the somewhat slower growth we have witnessed is a temporary phenomenon, and that economic activity is expected to bounce back to trend growth rates.	13	1
201504	1725	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	As mentioned by President Bullard, we also find that, historically, first-quarter GDP growth may not be adequately adjusted for seasonal variation.	13	1
201504	1726	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Over the past 30 years, seasonally adjusted growth in first-quarter GDP has averaged significantly less than growth over the remaining three quarters—1.8 percent as opposed to 2.7 percent.	13	1
201504	1727	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Additionally, if one examines the timing of the 10 weakest quarters over that span, six occurred in the first quarter and, 50 percent of the time, the weakest quarter was the first quarter of the year.	13	1
201504	1728	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Observations like these may point us to placing more weight on GDI—gross domestic income—or on GDPplus, which incorporates the income side of GDP, in interpreting current economic activity.	13	1
201504	1729	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	GDPplus has the advantage that, unlike GDI, it is available with the same timing as expenditure-side data.	13	1
201504	1730	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	With that as background, we continue to project that overall economic activity will accelerate modestly, that utilization gaps will continue to progressively narrow, and that inflation will continue to firm.	13	1
201504	1731	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	With a substantial amount of data forthcoming before our June meeting, I expect that we’ll have a clearer view of the emerging trends and confirmation—or not—that first-quarter economic performance was, indeed, a poor harbinger of the year to come.	13	1
201504	1732	2015-04-29	meeting	PRICHARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201504	1747	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	These reports suggest that it’s quite reasonable to expect a rebound in growth in Q2.	13	1
201504	1748	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	As usual, any discussion of labor markets these days turns quickly to the subject of shortages of skilled workers.	13	1
201504	1749	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	It does seem as if these shortages are having noticeable effects.	13	1
201504	1750	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	For example, the CEO of a large building materials company told of a trucking company refusing outgoing shipments due to a lack of drivers.	13	1

201504	1760	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Combined, these transitory factors could well explain a large part, if not all, of the first-quarter slowdown.	13	1

201504	1785	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	In agriculture, drought has worsened, although winter wheat production is shaping up to be similar to its recent five-year average, and corn and soybean planting intentions are generally strong.	13	1
201504	1786	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Still, persistently low crop prices and elevated input costs continue to increase farmers’ short-term financing needs.	13	1
201504	1787	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	For the national economy, my outlook for growth over the medium term is little changed.	13	1
201504	1788	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	I assume that the softness in first-quarter GDP proves transitory, as it has for the past several years.	13	1
201504	1789	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Weather effects and the sharp decline in energy investment look to be important factors holding down first-quarter growth.	13	1
201504	1790	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	However, more persistent factors producing softer export growth and a broader slowing in new investment bear watching.	13	1
201504	1791	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Alternatively, personal consumption appears to have some upside potential, as there is yet to be a significant boost to spending from lower gasoline prices.	13	1
201504	1792	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	March’s payroll numbers were clearly below expectations, though the labor market continues to improve in several aspects.	13	1
201504	1793	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	The services sector continues to perform well.	13	1
201504	1794	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	In the first quarter of this year, services added, on average, 179,000 additional jobs per month.	13	1
201504	1795	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	This is better than the 134,000 average in the first quarter of last year.	13	1
201504	1796	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Overall, I expect average monthly payroll growth to drift down from the rapid pace of 260,000 per month last year to 200,000 this year before settling down to a pace of around 150,000 next year as the labor market continues to tighten and growth moves to its trend level.	13	1
201504	1797	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Research by my staff also indicates improving labor market conditions, as reflected by the number of workers moving from lower- to higher-paying sectors—that is, the extent of cyclical upgrading.	13	1
201504	1798	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Their analysis shows that the rise in the quits rate over the past two years is primarily due to a higher quits rate in sectors with lower pay, like wholesale/retail trade and leisure/hospitality.	13	1
201504	1799	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Tracking individual workers in these sectors using Current Population Survey data shows that they have recently been moving into higher-paying sectors, like education and health services, at rates similar to those before the recession.	13	1
201504	1800	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Turning to inflation, I expect inflation to remain somewhat below target due to lagged effects from low energy prices and a stronger dollar.	13	1
201504	1801	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Inflation in some sectors, such as health care, have also notably slowed.	13	1
201504	1802	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	In addition, because of some of the current factors affecting inflation, year-over-year core could drift lower as stronger readings from a year ago—core PCE readings from March to June of last year were running at a solid 2 percent pace—drop out of the 12-month window.	13	1
201504	1803	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Such base effects have not affected my overall confidence regarding the medium-term outlook for inflation, assuming there are continued improvement in the labor market and stable longer-term expectations.	13	1

201504	1810	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will then turn to the national economy, where I see continued shortfalls in aggregate demand relative to supply.	13	1
201504	1811	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Labor markets are tight in many parts of the Ninth District, with contacts reporting significant swaths of unfilled openings.	13	1
201504	1812	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are signs of growing wage pressures in some economic sectors and geographical areas.	13	1

201504	1814	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Importantly, many businesses report having little or no ability to pass on wage increases to consumers in the form of price increases.	13	1
201504	1815	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So this is actually a flip side of the argument that President Williams offered—he indicated that he didn’t need to see wage increases to feel confident in the return of inflation to 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1816	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The flip side of this is that we might see wage increases happening and still not see inflation, because the question is whether those businesses experiencing the wage increases have the pricing power to pass that on.	13	1
201504	1817	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The contacts that we talked to in the intermeeting period suggested they do not.	13	1
201504	1818	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Contacts at businesses with an international footprint expressed concern during the intermeeting period about how the high value of the dollar would affect the demand for their products, especially in light of overall softness in global demand conditions.	13	1
201504	1819	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And some of the comments that President Bullard offered about China echo things we heard from our contacts during the intermeeting period.	13	1
201504	1820	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Not surprisingly, economic activity has slowed greatly in the oil-producing areas of North Dakota.	13	1

201504	1824	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Both real and nominal variables are telling us a similar story, I believe:  There is insufficient aggregate demand to use available resources in an effective fashion.	13	1
201504	1825	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In terms of real activity, if we look back over the past five years since the end of 2009, real GDP growth has averaged 2.3 percent.	13	1

201504	1827	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Here I’m using annual averages to get away from some of the seasonal adjustment factors that have been highlighted in previous interventions.	13	1
201504	1828	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This year now looks like it may be even worse than this weak average performance.	13	1
201504	1829	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In the wake of the data that we’ve received from the first quarter, Tealbook, Book A, has downgraded its outlook for 2015 economic activity, and it now projects growth in this calendar year to be less than 2 percent.	13	1

201504	1839	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Just to follow up on some of the discussion we heard earlier about inflation over the past few years, headline inflation did go above 2 percent in 2011, but I think if you go back over 2008, 2009, 2010, 2012, 2013, and 2014, in all of those years, it was below 2 percent.	13	1

201504	1842	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Market-based longer-term inflation expectations remain low.	13	1

201504	1844	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s absolutely true, I think, that if you do horse races of the kind that President Williams talked about, market-based measures don’t really fare that well.	13	1
201504	1845	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it’s actually because they’re of value to policymakers, not just to statistical forecasters, because we’re interested in a different loss function.	13	1
201504	1846	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We’re not interested in just minimizing mean squared error, we’re actually interested in trying to mitigate low inflation, especially when it’s going to be costly to households.	13	1
201504	1847	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I think that monitoring these market-based measures of inflation expectations is helpful along those lines.	13	1
201504	1848	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Tealbook, Book A, notes that we’ve seen some slippage in Michigan survey measures as well—I don’t want to put too much weight on that at this stage, but it’s something to watch.	13	1
201504	1849	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think there are signs in these data that we may be facing a prolonged period of inflation running substantially below our target.	13	1
201504	1850	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, why should we care about a possible downward slippage in inflation and inflation expectations?	13	1
201504	1851	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This builds on some of the remarks that President Rosengren already made, that persistently lower inflation expectations translate to lower nominal interest rates.	13	1
201504	1852	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Lower nominal interest rates translate into little recession-fighting capacity for the central bank.	13	1
201504	1853	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And such a reduction in policy capacity would be especially troubling because it would build on the policy capacity associated with a significant decline in the neutral real interest rate noted by President Rosengren.	13	1
201504	1854	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So this decline in inflation expectations really creates a first-order loss in terms of what we are trying to accomplish because it increases the probability of hitting up against the zero lower bound.	13	1
201504	1855	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As President Rosengren suggested, the decline in the neutral rate of interest should lead us to consider raising the inflation target.	13	1

201504	1857	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	More generally, I think that it would be useful to have discussions about the long-run goals that we have established.	13	1
201504	1858	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We learn more over time, and it’s useful to take that on board.	13	1
201504	1859	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We don’t want to do it every year, certainly, but I think at intervals we should be taking on information and reevaluating the longer-run goal statement.	13	1

201504	1861	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Every five years they go back and relook at their framework and what they’ve learned, and I think there’s something to be gained from that kind of systematic reevaluation of our framework.	13	1

201504	1874	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	On the one hand, I’m more uncertain about the growth outlook and more concerned about the potential downside risks to growth.	13	1
201504	1875	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	On the other hand, I think the downside risks on the inflation side have lessened considerably.	13	1
201504	1876	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	At the last meeting, I would have said that I expected further labor market improvement but was not yet reasonably confident that inflation would return to our 2 percent objective over the medium term.	13	1

201504	1878	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Conditional on further improvement in the labor market, I’m getting closer to being reasonably confident that inflation will return to our 2 percent objective, but I am less confident about when I will see further labor market improvement and how quickly it will occur.	13	1
201504	1879	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Turning first to the growth side, it strikes me that there are a lot of crosscurrents right now, and it’s really difficult to assess the net effect.	13	1
201504	1880	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	To me, this sharp slowing in payroll growth in March should not be a big surprise, however.	13	1
201504	1881	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	As I noted in my comments at the March FOMC meeting, a big gap had opened up between payroll growth and GDP growth, so unless one thought that the productivity growth trend would remain extraordinarily poor, it seemed reasonable to expect either GDP growth to pick up or payroll growth to slow down.	13	1
201504	1882	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	As it turns out, GDP growth slowed rather than picked up, so that left payroll growth to bear the brunt of the adjustment.	13	1
201504	1883	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	As I assess the first quarter, I do think there are both transitory and more persistent factors responsible for the slowdown.	13	1
201504	1884	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	On the transitory side, the Federal Reserve Bank of New York’s staff analysis does suggest that severe winter weather did have a sizable effect.	13	1

201504	1886	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Having said that, I wouldn’t take that point estimate too literally.	13	1
201504	1887	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, I do think the West Coast port slowdown did disrupt economic activity.	13	1
201504	1888	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	We had a small business advisory group meeting, and I was surprised that two out of the eight or nine businesses that attended referenced the port slowdown as hurting their businesses in the first quarter—this is around upstate New York and New Jersey, and that’s not really where I would expect the port slowdown to actually manifest itself, so I thought that was meaningful.	13	1

201504	1890	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	As Steve Kamin made clear, the dollar’s sharp appreciation has led to a deterioration in US net exports, and the Federal Reserve Bank of New York staff estimates that a 15 percent appreciation of the broad real trade- weighted dollar, which is a little bit more than we have right now in hand, would cut about 0.6 percentage point from the level of real GDP after one year, and I think that’s pretty consistent with the Tealbook estimates.	13	1
201504	1891	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	And I think it’s important because that’s just the partial equilibrium effect.	13	1
201504	1892	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	If you have a trade drag, it also drains real income from the economy, so there’s also the risk of second-order effects.	13	1
201504	1893	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, the second thing is that oil and gas investment is plunging.	13	1
201504	1894	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I’m not sure how severe the knock-on effects will be, but I don’t think we’re done yet.	13	1
201504	1895	2015-04-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Firms have been protected, in part, this year by hedging their future forward production by selling oil forward, and they’ll become more exposed to the current oil prices as time passes, so I expect a downward trend in investment to continue.	13	1

201504	1932	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	When you focus on recent growth rates, you get a fairly pessimistic picture of what’s happening, and we need to remind ourselves that the overall position of the economy is good.	13	1
201504	1933	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Let me start with the labor market.	13	1
201504	1934	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	The unemployment rate remains at 5.5 percent, a level that is not that much above many estimates of the natural rate today and is not above estimates of the natural rate that we had going into the March meeting.	13	1

201504	1937	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	So, from the policy viewpoint, the basic question on output and employment is whether employment will continue to grow at a rate sufficient to bring further improvement in the labor market in coming quarters.	13	1
201504	1938	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	The inflation rate remains below target, and that is a problem.	13	1
201504	1939	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	However, one gets the impression that while we are committed to looking through transitory factors—particularly dollar appreciation and the decline in oil prices, and gasoline prices in particular—popular discussion both here and in Europe has not focused on what that means.	13	1
201504	1940	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	It does not mean focusing solely on core inflation, because both energy and import prices affect core inflation.	13	1
201504	1941	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Core PCE inflation was 1.3 percent over the 12 months through March.	13	1
201504	1942	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	According to Board staff estimates in the Tealbook, increases in the dollar, which are very unlikely to persist indefinitely, are holding down that figure by 0.3 percentage point this year, bringing what could be called nontransitory core inflation to something like 1.6 percent.	13	1
201504	1943	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	And current energy price changes are reducing core inflation by another 0.1 percentage point, which brings you to a nontransitory core inflation rate of 1.7 percent.	13	1
201504	1944	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Now, you might be concerned about this if the low core numbers were being reflected in declining inflation expectations, but the survey measures seem to be relatively well anchored.	13	1
201504	1945	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	And all this, again, seems consistent with the data presented in the bottom two charts in Bill Wascher’s handout, which show inflation coming back rather quickly to just below 2 percent.	13	1
201504	1946	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I expect that the transitory factors—which are, fundamentally, oil and the exchange rate, which may or may not turn around reasonably soon—will gradually abate and possibly begin to reverse themselves.	13	1
201504	1947	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	We only need them to abate.	13	1
201504	1948	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	And labor market slack will continue to diminish, putting inflation on a path that, over the medium term, will take us very close to our inflation target.	13	1
201504	1949	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you.	13	1

201504	1951	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Tarullo.	13	1
201504	1952	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201504	1953	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	A lot of people have talked about Q1 and have posed the question, “Was Q1 an aberration?”	13	1
201504	1954	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I certainly hope that it was an aberration, and I expect that it has been an aberration.	13	1
201504	1955	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And I don’t think, personally, that we’re going to learn that much from the GDP number tomorrow morning.	13	1
201504	1956	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	If it’s kind of low or really low, it may not tell us very much because we had a really low number in the first quarter of 2014, and that didn’t seem to mean very much a quarter or two later.	13	1
201504	1957	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I actually think the question is not so much “Was Q1 an aberration?” but more the question that was implicit in some of Vice Chairman Dudley’s analysis a minute ago: “Are there things at work in the economy that will give us a growth path for the rest of the year that’s somewhat lower than what many of us expected toward the end of last year?”	13	1
201504	1958	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Not that it will in any way resemble Q1, but that somehow there are forces that are restraining growth a little bit more than we would have anticipated.	13	1
201504	1959	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Vice Chairman Dudley suggested one in his presentation.	13	1
201504	1960	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Another possible explanation of that phenomenon, should it occur, is the one that I think lies behind the Tealbook’s alternative scenario of a persistent slowdown—that is, that there is a kind of confidence problem that’s inhibiting consumer spending despite the extended improvement in income fundamentals because of job growth and maybe a little bit of wage growth, as well.	13	1
201504	1961	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I think for that reason one has to be a little agnostic right now in awaiting data over the next couple of months—as President Mester, Vice Chairman Dudley, and several other people have said.	13	1
201504	1962	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Although, as someone—I think it was Vice Chairman Dudley—also said, even then, it’s not clear how much some of the questions we’ve been asking will be clarified because there’s going to be a certain degree of snapback.	13	1
201504	1963	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	So instead of trying to speculate about all of that, I wanted to put on the table in a little bit more detail something Simon had an exhibit on in his presentation at the beginning of this meeting and that is the phenomenon of negative interest rates on European sovereign debt.	13	1
201504	1964	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	This phenomenon may be relevant to an assessment of the most likely medium-term US growth trend because of its potential effects on financial markets and the dollar, potential downside risks, and—and I’m not going to talk about this, but it may be relevant to the great debate about secular stagnation.	13	1
201504	1965	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	There have been negative interest rates on some shorter-term European sovereign debt for several years now, but the last six months or so have seen a qualitative change in the phenomenon in a number of respects.	13	1
201504	1966	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	First, negative rates have moved down along the yield curve in European countries with the strongest credit reputations to the point that, as in the chart that Simon distributed, we now see negative interest rates in a nontrivial portion of 5- to 7-year debt in the stronger European sovereigns.	13	1
201504	1967	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Second, yield curves, as he mentioned, are quite flat in these countries.	13	1
201504	1968	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I just checked this morning, and the spread between German 2-year and 10-year government bonds is about 40 basis points.	13	1
201504	1969	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	That compares to a spread of about 140 basis points for US Treasury securities.	13	1
201504	1970	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The 30-year bund is still trading positively but at 63 basis points, which is a pretty low number for a 30-year.	13	1
201504	1971	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	As everybody knows, the Swiss 10-year sovereign issue earlier this month went out at negative rates, and I believe that’s the first time in history that any sovereign debt of that duration has been priced negatively.	13	1
201504	1972	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And so, as far as Thomas Jordan knew, he was setting a precedent, or at least his country’s government was setting a precedent.	13	1
201504	1973	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Currently, as Simon’s chart shows, more than 35 percent of all euro-zone government debt carries a negative interest rate.	13	1
201504	1974	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	As recently as two weeks ago, I think, it was up over 40 percent.	13	1
201504	1975	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	There’s been a little bit of an interest rate change since then, which has affected the yields in some of those medium-term tenors.	13	1
201504	1976	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Substantial portions of some non-euro-zone sovereigns, notably Denmark and Switzerland, also are priced at negative rates, and some shorter-duration corporate bonds of the highly rated European firms are also priced at negative rates now.	13	1
201504	1977	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Clearly, this is all related to long-standing European economic problems—and, more immediately, first the prospect and now the reality of the ECB’s QE program.	13	1
201504	1978	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	It’s also related, I guess, to the relative scarcity of sovereign debt instruments issued by countries like Germany that continue to run tight fiscal policies and large current account surpluses, notwithstanding economic conditions in the euro zone more generally.	13	1
201504	1979	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The unprecedented nature of this phenomenon has caused most thoughtful analysts to offer only tentative views of the relative importance of various stories that explain why investors would hold longer-term sovereigns with negative yields.	13	1
201504	1980	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Logically, I think the explanations fall into three categories.	13	1
201504	1981	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	First is gloom about the long-term prospects for the countries in question.	13	1
201504	1982	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Now, it’s true, there’s not a whole lot of high optimism to be found about European growth prospects, but almost everybody discounts this as a dominant explanation—that is, the idea that there’s going to be negative growth or deflation for 10 years in Europe.	13	1
201504	1983	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The second group of explanations are based on investor inertia, basically—whether forced, as in the case of pension funds and some other institutions, which may be required by law or regulation to hold certain percentages of extremely low-risk bonds, or unforced, as in the case of other investors who are themselves unsure of what all of this negative rate stuff means and are waiting right now to see what happens, and maybe change their investment decision if things don’t turn around relatively soon.	13	1
201504	1984	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The third set of explanations is that, in an environment that doesn’t offer a lot of appealing investment options, there may be some plays available here.	13	1
201504	1985	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	For example, you may get capital gains if yields go even further into negative territory, or you may get exchange rate gains whether from short-term developments that cause quick swings in currencies or from longer-term appreciations of currencies with very low inflation or even deflation.	13	1
201504	1986	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	That is, if you think that there’s going to be very low inflation in Switzerland for a long time to come and you are a US investor, and you think that there’s going to be inflation in the United States, you might actually end up profiting by that play.	13	1
201504	1987	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	As an example of this search for an investment play, I took note of one analyst’s advice to his clients that the best play may be in bonds with a good bit of convexity so as to maximize the chances of catching the wave of large market movements when these trends reverse.	13	1
201504	1988	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	To date, as a very good Federal Reserve Bank of New York MarketSource report indicated, there have been relatively few discontinuities observed in European debt markets.	13	1
201504	1989	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	But I think this is the point at which Herb Stein’s memorable aphorism comes to mind, because unless the medium-term prospects for Europe are a lot worse than most people think, there’s going to be a reversal of negative rates at some point.	13	1
201504	1990	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Even if there’s no near-term reversal resulting from sufficiently enhanced growth prospects or from sufficiently significant policy moves, a nontrivial number of investors might, over time, overcome their inertia and start seeking positive nominal yields elsewhere.	13	1
201504	1991	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	There are a lot of potential outcomes here, some of which are relatively benign.	13	1
201504	1992	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Others, though, are of potentially greater concern.	13	1
201504	1993	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	For example, if enough investors were caught flat- footed by an unexpected change in ECB policy—and, paradoxically, an unexpected improvement in European economic prospects—there could be some pretty significant turmoil in relevant markets, although there we would have to ask ourselves how much the financial turmoil would be offset by the prospect of improvement in the real economy.	13	1
201504	1994	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I think of even greater concern to us would be a decision by those investors who did not initially move out of European sovereign debt that they couldn’t take the uncertainty or the negative rates any longer.	13	1
201504	1995	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The natural destination for these investors is, of course, US Treasury securities, and I think that some of the dollar strengthening we’ve seen this year has been attributable to the existence of investors who have already made such a move.	13	1
201504	1996	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And it is possible, though very hard to judge how likely, that many more could follow.	13	1
201504	1997	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	If they were to do so, the recent modest retracing by the dollar could itself be halted and reversed pretty significantly.	13	1
201504	1998	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	On this last possibility, it’s worth mentioning that, notwithstanding the notable spreads between bunds and Treasury securities, some European investors have apparently concluded that the roughly 80 basis point difference between German and US two-year bond rates is insufficient to compensate for currency-hedging costs and other risks.	13	1
201504	1999	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And this calculus is, I think, what may be contributing to some of the otherwise-hard-to-explain changes in the yield curves of some of the stronger European sovereigns, since fundamentals are not really changing there but you’re seeing some movement in those yield curves, which I take to be everybody repositioning, trying to figure out the best defensive place to be right now.	13	1
201504	2000	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	So investors may be moving around within bonds that are either euro-denominated or effectively tied to the euro.	13	1
201504	2001	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	But if that spread between US Treasury interest rates and rates on European sovereigns were to widen, either because we raise rates or because QE and other developments in the EU drove yields down even further there, then more European investors might be tempted to take the Atlantic plunge.	13	1
201504	2002	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And this, in turn, could cause some market dislocation in Europe, a renewed unwelcome strengthening of the dollar, or both.	13	1
201504	2003	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	This is pretty speculative right now, but certainly in a context in which the trajectory of US economic performance is at least in some question after the past few months, I think this situation is another reason for us not to be in a hurry to raise rates, at least not until we’ve had a chance to see how investors are adapting, and, most importantly, what their adaptation means for the dollar.	13	1

201504	2039	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201504	2040	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	I wanted to reflect briefly on the quarterly assessment of financial stability and then turn to the outlook.	13	1
201504	2041	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Although overall risks to financial stability in the United States remain moderate, one area that bears heightened scrutiny is the potential for spikes in volatility due to changes in market liquidity.	13	1
201504	2042	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In particular, we should be asking whether episodes of severe volatility, like the wild swings we saw in Treasury bond yields on the morning of October 15, are likely to become the new normal.	13	1
201504	2043	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Bond inventories held by broker-dealers have declined, in some part due to regulatory changes.	13	1
201504	2044	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Separately, and predating the onset of Dodd-Frank, changes in market structure may be at least as important in some markets, for which automated and high- frequency traders may now account for a majority of the activity.	13	1
201504	2045	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Coupled with the big run-up we’ve seen of investments in bond mutual funds, this raises the specter of amplification of volatility and fire-sale dynamics at times when investors looking to cash out could be surprised by lower-than-expected liquidity.	13	1
201504	2046	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Together, these changes to the structure of financial markets could increase the volatility of asset price responses.	13	1
201504	2047	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	A modest increase in the average level of volatility shouldn’t be a concern, particularly if market participants respond with more rigorous risk management.	13	1
201504	2048	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	But a lack of liquidity that leads to large and self-reinforcing swings in prices and market freezes during periods of stress could pose threats to financial stability and would be especially concerning in the case of the US Treasury market, with its traditional benchmark status.	13	1
201504	2049	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	I think it’s premature to draw conclusions for financial stability.	13	1
201504	2050	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Some of the preoccupation with the decline in dealer inventories may, in fact, be misplaced, and I think focusing too much attention on the critical role of dealers as reliable providers of liquidity during severe stress episodes suggests a very high degree of amnesia.	13	1
201504	2051	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Moreover, bid-ask spreads are in line with historical norms.	13	1
201504	2052	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	As we’re learning from the investigation of October 15, we also do not yet fully understand the implications of the growing role of high-frequency traders in these markets.	13	1
201504	2053	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	With heterogeneity across their size, business models, and trading strategies, some of their activities may, in fact, contribute to market liquidity, while some may be destabilizing.	13	1
201504	2054	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	And, finally, it’s unclear how much leverage or high-quality liquidity is embedded in bond mutual funds and how vulnerable they might be to liquidity mismatch and fire-sale dynamics.	13	1
201504	2055	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	The Dodd-Frank requirement for mutual funds to undertake stress tests holds some promise, but only if deployed rigorously.	13	1
