201504	2077	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Average monthly payroll employment gains were 200,000, down about 60,000 from last year’s pace, and all of the indicators of slack have moved sideways in the last month.	13	1
201504	2078	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Of course, there’s a danger in reading too much into this data.	13	1
201504	2079	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	It’s entirely possible we’ll see the stronger trend in underlying activity that was apparent in the second half of the year reassert itself.	13	1
201504	2080	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	But it’s important to keep in mind the international context.	13	1
201504	2081	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	On the one hand, we have seen some encouraging data from the euro area—data on retail sales and industrial production, in particular—which holds out some promise that aggregate demand will strengthen.	13	1
201504	2082	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	And the dollar’s sharp ascent seems to have stalled, at least for now.	13	1
201504	2083	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	On the other hand, negotiations between Greece and its creditors remain challenging.	13	1
201504	2084	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	And although the macro data are notoriously unreliable, Chinese GDP growth looks to have slowed noticeably in the first quarter.	13	1
201504	2085	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	On balance, the foreign economic outlook suggests continued weak demand in some important advanced foreign economies and signs of faltering growth in some important emerging market economies.	13	1
201504	2086	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In this context, the threat to price stability from stronger demand growth in the United States seems quite limited.	13	1
201504	2087	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Let me close by touching briefly on inflation.	13	1
201504	2088	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Recent data have provided some reassurance that underlying inflation is not moving lower.	13	1
201504	2089	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Oil prices have moved up somewhat further since March, and the 3-month moving average of core PCE inflation has increased from the very low level reached in January.	13	1
201504	2090	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Even so, current estimates of both the 3- and 12-month changes in core PCE prices point to an underlying trend rate of only 1.4 percent.	13	1
201504	2091	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	And while it’s reassuring that survey-based measures of inflation expectations are remaining stable, and there has been some improvement in market-based measures, the latter are still materially below the levels that prevailed until the middle of last year.	13	1
201504	2092	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	The overall picture should suggest at least some caution about attributing all of the recent softening to transitory factors and puts a high premium on incoming data as we assess the timing of liftoff, which is the topic for tomorrow’s discussion.	13	1

201504	2214	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Thank you, Madam Chair.	21	2
201504	2215	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	I’ll be referring to the handout labelled “Material for Briefing on Monetary Policy Alternatives.”	21	2
201504	2216	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	At previous meetings and, indeed, yesterday, several of you discussed the possibility of market turbulence in the run-up to, or following, the onset of policy tightening.	21	2
201504	2217	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Although the Committee now has communications tools, including the SEP, that it did not have at the start of previous tightening cycles, unexpected movements in yields and volatility, up or down, may nevertheless occur even if the expected policy rate path does not shift markedly.	21	2
201504	2218	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The upper-left panel of your first exhibit provides some perspective by showing the staff’s estimate of the 10-year Treasury term premium over the past 25 years.	21	2
201504	2219	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The vertical bars highlight three episodes:  the beginning of the tightening cycles in 1994 and 2004 and the taper tantrum in 2013.	21	2
201504	2220	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Within the shaded 18-month windows around these events, term premiums moved substantially.	21	2
201504	2221	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	But they also did so at other times—and likely in response to factors other than monetary policy.	21	2
201504	2222	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The upper-right panel isolates the episodes associated with the three shaded regions and, for reference, the current episode conditional on the staff’s assumption of liftoff at the September meeting.	21	2
201504	2223	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	As shown by the red line, in 1994, the term premium rose substantially in the months following the onset of tightening.	21	2
201504	2224	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	By contrast, in 2004, in blue, it moved up several months before the federal funds rate in response to stronger economic data that moved up the expected timing of policy tightening.	21	2
201504	2225	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	But the term premium then began to decline and continued to do so over the remainder of 2004—the well-known “conundrum” episode.	21	2
201504	2226	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Finally, in 2013, the term premium began to rise around the time of Chairman Bernanke’s JEC testimony in late May, apparently reflecting market participants’ reassessment of the outlook for asset purchases and possibly policy rates.	21	2
201504	2227	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	As shown in the middle-left panel, these term premium movements may, to some extent, have reflected uncertainty about the future course of monetary policy.	21	2
201504	2228	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The panel shows the width of the 90 percent confidence interval for the federal funds rate 12 months ahead, derived from options on federal funds futures.	21	2
201504	2229	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The term premiums are highly correlated with this proxy for policy uncertainty in the 1994 and 2004 episodes, but less so in 2013, presumably because uncertainty in that episode was driven less by the path of the federal funds rate over the next 12 months than by the course of asset purchases and longer-term rates.	21	2
201504	2230	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	These experiences suggest that, even in a context in which the FOMC’s policy communications tools are more extensive than in the 1994 and 2004 cycles, it may be prudent for the Committee to consider its likely response to unexpected changes in term premiums that might occur around the time when it begins policy firming.	21	2
201504	2231	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	A special exhibit in the “Monetary Policy Strategies” section of the Tealbook, Book B, provides some background that might be useful.	21	2
201504	2232	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	As shown by the dotted black line in the middle-right panel, the staff projects that the 10-year term premium will rise at a modest pace after the Committee is assumed to start raising the federal funds rate.	21	2
201504	2233	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	This slow projected rise in the term premium reflects in part the waning effects on longer-term yields of the Committee’s asset holdings.	21	2
201504	2234	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The magenta line presents the path for the term premium in a scenario, labeled “Tightening Tantrum,” that is similar in magnitude to, albeit less persistent than, the rise in the term premium that began in May 2013 (the green line).	21	2
201504	2235	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	As shown in the lower-left panel, in the “tantrum” case, optimal control would call for a more gradual increase in the federal funds rate for several quarters, with the federal funds rate running, on average, 35 basis points below the optimal control path associated with the Tealbook baseline (the black line) over the four years following the shock.	21	2
201504	2236	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Recent public discourse has largely focused on the specter of another market tantrum.	21	2
201504	2237	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	However, a repeat of the 2004 experience, in which term premiums remained surprisingly low for a while, is also a possibility.	21	2
201504	2238	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	For example, such an outcome might occur through the effects of the very large asset purchase programs that are under way in Europe and Japan.	21	2
201504	2239	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The yellow line in the middle-right panel shows a scenario for the path of the term premium that is similar to the 2004 conundrum episode.	21	2
201504	2240	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In this instance, optimal control would call for a federal funds rate path that is 40 basis points higher, on average, over the next four years.	21	2
201504	2241	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The lower-right panel highlights a few caveats.	21	2
201504	2242	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The tantrum scenario abstracts from the possibility of widespread liquidity or solvency problems, a point worth mentioning in light of recent commentary about reduced liquidity in key markets, which Simon discussed in his briefing.	21	2
201504	2243	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	More generally, the scenarios do not examine the consequences of term premium movements that are associated with other disturbances.	21	2
201504	2244	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	If, for example, the term premium fell because of weaker economic activity abroad and associated flight-to-safety flows, the optimal policy response could be to keep the federal funds rate lower, not higher, than in the baseline.	21	2
201504	2245	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Finally, the appropriate policy response to either scenario depends importantly on the persistence of the deviation of the term premium from its baseline path, which will be known only in retrospect.	21	2
201504	2246	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In response to such scenarios, policymakers may wish to emphasize that they will respond to financial conditions that are judged inconsistent with the achievement of the FOMC’s objectives.	21	2
201504	2247	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Turning to the draft policy alternatives, thanks to the excellent work of Bill Wascher and his colleagues, I can discuss the alternatives as distributed yesterday.	21	2
201504	2248	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	A key issue for your decision today is how to convey the implications of recent and incoming economic information for the likely timing of policy normalization.	21	2
201504	2249	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	First, as indicated at the top of exhibit 2, assuming that the Committee decides to remove time-based forward guidance from the statement, it will primarily rely on paragraphs 1 and 2 to speak to its assessment of whether its criteria for liftoff have been met or when they are likely to be met.	21	2
201504	2250	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In broad terms, with alternative B, the Committee would communicate that the conditions for an increase in the federal funds rate are not yet in place, while alternative C would suggest that those conditions will likely be in place in the near future.	21	2
201504	2251	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Alternative A would indicate that the preconditions for liftoff are unlikely to be met in the near future.	21	2
201504	2252	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The second set of bullets summarizes how each of the three alternatives may shape expectations for the timing of liftoff.	21	2
201504	2253	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	On the Committee’s criterion that it would need to see “further improvement in the labor market,” alternative B states that “underutilization of labor resources was little changed” over the intermeeting period, citing the moderation in the pace of job gains and the steady unemployment rate.	21	2
201504	2254	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Regarding the second criterion, that the Committee would need to be “reasonably confident that inflation will move back to its 2 percent objective over the medium term,” alternative B updates the description of recent data in paragraph 1 and retains, in paragraph 2, the expectation that inflation will “rise gradually toward 2 percent over the medium term.”	21	2
201504	2255	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Thus, alternative B does not signal a change in confidence about the inflation outlook.	21	2
201504	2256	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In addition, the Committee noted in the March minutes that the timing of the first increase in the federal funds rate would also depend on the evolution of economic conditions and the outlook.	21	2
201504	2257	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In that regard, alternative B provides the judgment that the Committee sees the recent weakness in economic activity either “in part” or “at least in part” as transitory and indicates that, despite the first-quarter slowdown, the Committee still expects economic growth at a pace that will lead to further improvement in labor market conditions.	21	2
201504	2258	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Under alternative B, the Committee would remain fully data dependent and offer no time-based guidance about how soon the conditions for liftoff are likely to be met.	21	2
201504	2259	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	By contrast, alternative C would communicate the Committee’s readiness to begin policy normalization in the near term.	21	2
201504	2260	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Paragraph 1 indicates that, despite a steady jobless rate, “some labor market indicators suggest that underutilization of labor resources continued to diminish.”	21	2
201504	2261	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	It expresses a more positive reading of recent news on inflation and notes that, although inflation remained below the Committee’s objective, “it was no longer declining.”	21	2
201504	2262	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In addition, paragraph 1 of alternative C attributes this winter’s economic slowdown largely to transitory factors.	21	2
201504	2263	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Paragraph 2 suggests not only that the Committee continues to anticipate moderate economic growth and further improvement in labor market conditions, but also that it now has become more (or perhaps somewhat more) confident that inflation “will rise gradually to 2 percent over the medium term.”	21	2
201504	2264	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Having made those assessments of recent developments and the outlook, the Committee would adopt new guidance in paragraph 3.	21	2
201504	2265	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Rather than repeating the criteria for liftoff adopted in the March statement, the Committee would announce that “economic conditions may” or “likely will soon warrant an increase in the target range for the federal funds rate.”	21	2
201504	2266	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	While alternative C reintroduces a time dimension to the forward guidance, it also retains the more general data-dependent statement that the timing of the adjustment would depend on realized and expected progress toward the Committee’s objectives of maximum employment and 2 percent inflation.	21	2
201504	2267	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Finally, with alternative A, the Committee would express heightened concerns about the underlying strength of the economic expansion, downside risks to the outlook for the labor market, and possible persistence of below-target inflation.	21	2
201504	2268	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In paragraph 1, in alternative A, as in alternative B, the Committee describes underutilization of labor resources as “little changed,” but it stresses that inflation “continued to run well below the Committee’s longer-run objective.”	21	2
201504	2269	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	It does not offer any judgment on how much of this winter’s slowdown may have been transitory.	21	2
201504	2270	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Additionally, in paragraph 2’s assessment of the outlook, under alternative A it would be reported that the Committee now sees the risks to economic activity and the labor market as “tilted to the downside.”	21	2
201504	2271	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	And the statement would voice concern that “inflation could run substantially below”	21	2
201504	2272	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Given these risks to the outlook, alternative A introduces a somewhat more stringent precondition for the initiation of policy firming, stating in paragraph 3 that the Committee would need to see inflation “clearly moving up toward” the Committee’s 2 percent objective rather than being “reasonably confident” that it will do so.	21	2
201504	2273	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	In addition, alternative A emphasizes concern about the inflation outlook by indicating that “the Committee is prepared to use all of its tools as necessary to return inflation to 2 percent within one to two years.”	21	2
201504	2274	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	All told, alternative A would communicate that liftoff appears unlikely for some time.	21	2
201504	2275	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Thank you, Madam Chair.	21	2
201504	2276	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	That concludes my prepared remarks, and I will be glad to take any questions.	21	2

201504	2328	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Thomas, I wonder if you could, to help us understand these scenarios, refresh the Committee’s memory about the extent to which overnight short-term interest rates that we influence directly through the federal funds rate target can affect economic activity in the FRB/US model independently of the values of the 5-, 7-, and 10-year interest rates, which appear to figure prominently in these scenarios.	22	2
201504	2329	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Monetary policy transmission in the FRB/US model is largely through long-term rates, so I’m—let me think.	22	2
201504	2330	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Entirely?	22	2

201504	2335	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	By implication, these shifts in term premiums that we are considering in the scenarios are actually quite powerful.	22	2

201504	2340	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Have we been wrong for years?	22	2

201504	2343	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	There are two separate aspects to this.	22	2
201504	2344	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	One is, what if monetary policy transmission also importantly works through term premiums?	22	2
201504	2345	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	That means that term premiums systematically vary with the path of short-term interest rates.	22	2

201504	2347	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	So to the extent that choices about the path of the funds rate affect the outlook for future output gaps, that has an added effect by moving term premiums endogenously as well.	22	2
201504	2348	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	A separate question is, should a Taylor rule also take into account other financial conditions more broadly?	22	2
201504	2349	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	And there, arguably, a Taylor rule that responds only to current economic conditions may have a shortcoming because it responds only to current inflation and, say, the current output gap, whereas financial conditions that you observe today, like movements in the dollar and things like that, clearly affect your outlook in the future.	22	2
201504	2350	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	So that is not an aspect that is captured by Taylor rules that just mechanically respond to current conditions.	22	2
201504	2351	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Of course, for example, if you look at optimal control, it obviously knows about these things because it’s run under perfect foresight.	22	2
201504	2352	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Therefore, agents anticipate that changes in financial conditions today more broadly, including changes in term premiums, will affect the future outlook.	22	2

201504	2379	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	But if that had recently occurred, then, indeed, there is a little bit of a discrepancy in terms of your standard Taylor rule response only to current conditions.	22	2
201504	2380	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	If these current conditions haven’t responded yet to that decline in the term premium, then there is a bit of a tension since you project that that decline in the term premium, in itself, actually is going to provide a boost.	22	2
201504	2381	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	If you think, for example, about the staff projection, it is, very importantly, driven by our assessment of financial conditions today and over the projection period.	22	2
201504	2382	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	So the Taylor rule does take account of that over the projection period.	22	2
201504	2383	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Fischer, do you have a two-hander?	22	2
201504	2384	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Yes, it’s a two-hander.	22	2
201504	2385	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	What is the correlation between the term premium and the difference between the short-term rate and the long-term rate?	22	2
201504	2386	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Is it high?	22	2
201504	2387	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	The correlation between the term premium and the slope—sadly, I do not know this right off the top of—	22	2
201504	2388	2015-04-29	meeting	POTTER	It has to be high on a daily basis.	22	2
201504	2389	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It has to be high.	22	2

201504	2393	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Williams.	22	2
201504	2394	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	My question is related to President Lacker’s topic, but I guess it’s really more about how we think about movements in the term premium in general.	22	2
201504	2395	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	As I understand the FRB/US model and the way you described it, Thomas, is that these movements have very powerful effects because they affect all asset prices.	22	2
201504	2396	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	They affect mortgage rates, the stock market, and the exchange rate.	22	2
201504	2397	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	These movements are really spilling over throughout financial conditions, and so have a very powerful effect on GDP, employment, and inflation.	22	2
201504	2398	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The research on the effects of QE—I’m thinking of the work of Vissing-Jorgensen and Krishnamurthy—a lot of the research has looked at this and seems to have called into question whether there’s this full pass-through of Treasury term premium effects onto private rates, such as corporate bond rates and mortgage rates, as well as asset prices in general.	22	2
201504	2399	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I guess the question is, to what extent are you buying into the story that maybe the term premium doesn’t affect financial conditions as fully as the FRB/US model would tell you?	22	2
201504	2400	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Or how do we think about some of the more recent research in terms of these simulations?	22	2
201504	2401	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	I think the short answer to that is, what I have here on the handout and what is being shown in the “Monetary Policy Strategies” section of Tealbook, Book B, of course, buys fully into the FRB/US view that, to the extent that long rates matter for real activity, changes in the term premium are in long-term rates.	22	2
201504	2402	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	So no distinction is drawn between whether the movement is in the expectations component or in the term-premium component.	22	2
201504	2403	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	We could certainly try to look at how the results will change.	22	2
201504	2404	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Broadly speaking, my expectation would be that the lines you see in the lower-left panel are based on 100 percent—that is, term premiums are worth exactly as much as changes in expected future interest rates.	22	2
201504	2405	2015-04-29	meeting	LAUBACH	Then you can shave that if you thought that a more reasonable estimate was, say, 50 percent.	22	2
201504	2406	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	22	2
201504	2407	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The second comment is really to President Lacker.	22	2
201504	2408	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This is why you have a time-varying r*.	22	2
201504	2409	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In my view, this is the argument for thinking about the fact that the term premium changes over decades.	22	2
201504	2410	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Other factors, such as risk premiums, the equity premium, and the sovereign risk premium—lots of things change in the economy, which is basically the argument not to have a constant r*.	22	2
201504	2411	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Your point is exactly right.	22	2
201504	2412	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	On its own, the term premium going down would argue for a higher r*.	22	2
201504	2413	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	So what’s really striking is that Laubach-Williams, which just tries to let the data decide, has r* falling all of the way to zero, despite the fact that QE and other factors have pushed the term premium down.	22	2
201504	2414	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The way I view this is, there are these factors pushing r* up, but the other factors, the headwinds and the other things, are just so much more powerful that, on net, they push r* down.	22	2
201504	2415	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Through the lens of r*, everything makes sense.	22	2

201504	2426	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My rationale for alternative A is based on the modal outlook for prices and employment, the risks to that outlook, and the zero lower bound.	23	2
201504	2427	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On the benchmark outlook in the Tealbook, Book A, you see that inflation does not return to the FOMC’s target of 2 percent until 2019, which is four years from now.	23	2
201504	2428	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We should take steps to facilitate a faster return of inflation to target.	23	2
201504	2429	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These additional steps would also help boost employment over the medium term.	23	2
201504	2430	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Our congressional mandate calls for us to achieve maximum employment and stable prices.	23	2
201504	2431	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we are able to simultaneously create more jobs and improve our performance on the price-stability goal, we should do so.	23	2
201504	2432	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s the argument just based on the modal outlook alone.	23	2
201504	2433	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It provides a clear justification for the policy course described in alternative A.	23	2
201504	2434	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think this argument is enhanced by consideration of the risks to the outlook.	23	2
201504	2435	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I agree with the Tealbook, Book A—the risks to the outlook for prices and employment are to the downside.	23	2
201504	2436	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The standard risk-management considerations that are often advanced by the Committee suggest that these future downside risks bolster the case for current accommodation.	23	2
201504	2437	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	When we think about the zero lower bound, as recent Brookings conference paper by President Evans and his colleagues makes crystal clear, these risk-management considerations are significantly enhanced by the presence of the zero lower bound.	23	2
201504	2438	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The past five years have taught us that when we’re close to the zero lower bound, as we expect to be over the medium term, we will not be able to mitigate downside risks as effectively as we would like.	23	2
201504	2439	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As President Evans’s paper points out, this means that we should make policy choices so as to strengthen the economy in advance of these shocks hitting.	23	2
201504	2440	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That, again, argues in favor of the kind of stance described in alternative A.	23	2
201504	2441	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I see three arguments in favor of alternative A—the modal outlook, the risks to the outlook, and the constraints on policy imposed by the zero lower bound.	23	2
201504	2442	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I’ve indicated in the past, I believe that it is important for monetary policy decisions to take account of the macroeconomic risks created by potential financial instability.	23	2
201504	2443	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My own reading of the QS report is that the main financial stability risk we face right now is actually associated with higher, not lower, interest rates.	23	2
201504	2444	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This will build on some of the work that Thomas was showing us earlier this morning.	23	2
201504	2445	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In May 2013, we began to tighten policy while we were still apparently several years away from achieving our macroeconomic objectives.	23	2
201504	2446	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Market participants lost confidence in the willingness of the FOMC to buffer the economy against adverse shocks, and we saw a rapid increase in the term premium.	23	2
201504	2447	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we raise the target range for the federal funds rate when headline inflation and core inflation are both running so low, we risk sending the same message that we did in 2013, and, accordingly, we risk seeing the same rapid run-up in the term premium.	23	2
201504	2448	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Madam Chair, the modal outlook for inflation is too low.	23	2
201504	2449	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We can best address this deficiency and simultaneously create more jobs by making clear, as alternative A does, that we are willing to use all of our tools to return inflation to target within one to two years, and that such a determined approach in the pursuit of our mandated objectives is also the best way to manage the risks we face, especially near the zero lower bound.	23	2
201504	2450	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’d like to conclude by suggesting a topic for a possible research briefing.	23	2

201504	2452	2015-04-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it would be really useful for us to understand the work going on in the System on r*—what’s determining it and what’s moving it around.	23	2

201504	2546	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	We will also have two new reports on trimmed mean PCE inflation and household inflation expectations.	23	2
201504	2547	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Several of the current uncertainties hanging over the world economy may be at least partially resolved.	23	2
201504	2548	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	It seems to me to be well within the realm of possibility that the information we receive between now and June will warrant a policy response.	23	2
201504	2549	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	I believe it is important, therefore, that a June liftoff remain on the table.	23	2
201504	2550	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	While, technically, there is nothing in the language of alternative B that takes June liftoff off the table, I do have some concern.	23	2
201504	2551	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	I can readily imagine people in the financial markets and business press drawing the conclusion that June action is all but impossible, given the lack of foreshadowing in alternative B.	23	2
201504	2552	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	This move in market expectations could prove to be an impediment to Committee action.	23	2
201504	2553	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Therefore, the language in paragraph 3 of alternative C with the “may soon” option is attractive, as it more clearly indicates that June remains a viable option.	23	2
201504	2554	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	On the other hand, alternative B states the Committee’s intention to take timely action once its criteria have been met.	23	2
201504	2555	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	With the possibility of continued uncertainty on both fronts, I support alternative B.	23	2
201504	2556	2015-04-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Thank you.	23	2

201504	2558	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Tarullo.	23	2
201504	2559	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2560	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I support alternative B, although with a language change, which I will get to toward the end of my remarks.	23	2
201504	2561	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I first wanted to say that I agree with people that once we have removed the forward guidance, as we’ve now done, every meeting should be a live meeting in which the possibility of a change in the federal funds rate can be on the table.	23	2
201504	2562	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Having said that, though, we still have different assessments as to how likely it is that we think we’re going to want to raise the federal funds rate target range in the next couple of meetings.	23	2
201504	2563	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I, for one, don’t think it’s very likely at all.	23	2
201504	2564	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	But I do believe that we should not do anything that formally takes it off the table.	23	2
201504	2565	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I wasn’t going to comment on this, but President Lockhart’s thoughtful observations did provoke me to say a couple of things on how to begin moving forward.	23	2
201504	2566	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I think I’m coming out in a somewhat different place, although only in a tentative way, and would like to hear other people’s views on this.	23	2
201504	2567	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I’d be reluctant to put back into paragraph 3 at some point an indication that, one or two meetings from now, we are more likely to move.	23	2
201504	2568	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The reason is, I think that becomes a form of calendar guidance of its own.	23	2
201504	2569	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Just as, when we put in the quasi-calendar guidance before, saying, “Well, this isn’t really calendar—it’s all data dependent,” the markets all read it as calendar guidance, I think the same thing is going to happen now if we put in language such as that included in paragraph 3 of alternative C.	23	2
201504	2570	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	My preference would be that we use paragraph 2 as the vehicle for beginning to communicate a change in the Committee’s collective view as to where the economy is headed, which, it’s hoped, actually begins to, I would say, increase some volatility a little bit as people begin to think liftoff is more likely but is not a done deal.	23	2
201504	2571	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	That way, we don’t have a surprise at the meeting in which we eventually lift the target range, but it is not de facto occurring just by the inclusion of language in paragraph 3 at a meeting before we’ve actually decided to lift the target range.	23	2
201504	2572	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	As I said, my current view is that June is very unlikely as an appropriate time to increase the target range.	23	2
201504	2573	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I thought that paragraph 1 as originally circulated a week or two ago actually hit about right the factual characterization of the economy.	23	2
201504	2574	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	It’s fairly hard to argue with the proposition that the data were rather disappointing and pretty bad, yet paragraph 1 began with something saying that it was “in part reflecting transitory factors.”	23	2
201504	2575	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Most important, I think, the beginning of paragraph 2 communicates that “Although growth in output and employment slowed during the first quarter, the Committee continues to expect…”	23	2
201504	2576	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	In other words, the basic direction of the Committee’s expectations hasn’t changed, notwithstanding the disappointing developments reported in paragraph 1.	23	2
201504	2577	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Notwithstanding the fact that I thought the original paragraph 1 got it about right, I’m okay with most of the changes that have been made since the original language was circulated, although I wouldn’t want to see us going too much further to try to look for the cheery in a way that would obscure what’s actually happened.	23	2
201504	2578	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	The one exception, Madam Chair, is the sentence on inflation, which is the second-to-last sentence in paragraph 1.	23	2
201504	2579	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Back in March, that sentence said “Inflation has declined further below the Committee’s longer-run objective, largely reflecting declines in energy prices.”	23	2
201504	2580	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	So the phrase “largely reflecting declines in energy prices” was, in effect, explaining how the decline had gone further below.	23	2
201504	2581	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	It was an explanation of the delta.	23	2
201504	2582	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I think the language that was circulated originally to the Committee a week or two ago is now in alternative A, and the language is as follows: “Inflation continued to run well below the Committee’s longer-run objective, largely reflecting earlier declines in energy prices and decreasing prices of non-energy imports.”	23	2
201504	2583	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	There, the way I read it when that language was circulated was that now this phrase “largely reflecting” in effect explains the “well” part—why it is “well” below.	23	2
201504	2584	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	But as it’s been modified, the sentence now reads “Inflation continued to run below the Committee’s longer-run objective, largely reflecting earlier declines in energy prices and decreasing prices of non-energy imports.”	23	2
201504	2585	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t think it’s the case that all of the difference between the Committee’s target and where we are right now is explained based on the earlier declines in energy prices and decreasing prices of non-energy imports.	23	2
201504	2586	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	On the contrary, as many people pointed out yesterday and a little bit today already, we haven’t been hitting the inflation target for quite some time now.	23	2
201504	2587	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	And if you pull out housing inflation—which, of course, is largely a function of rents imputed to homeowners—then you have an awful lot of continuing questions about how much inflation there is.	23	2
201504	2588	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	As a factual matter, I think we should modify that sentence to say something like the following: “Inflation continued to run below the Committee’s longer-run objective, partly reflecting earlier declines in energy prices and decreasing prices of non-energy imports”	23	2
201504	2589	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	—or something just making clear that that second phrase does not account for all of the difference between the Committee’s target and where we are right now.	23	2
201504	2590	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Finally, like those who have gone before me, I would prefer removing “at least” in the first sentence.	23	2
201504	2591	2015-04-29	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you.	23	2
201504	2592	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	23	2
201504	2593	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Lacker.	23	2
201504	2594	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2595	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Our March statement provided forward guidance only as far ahead as this meeting, and it effectively said we were going to go to a meeting-by-meeting, data-dependent approach to decisionmaking.	23	2
201504	2596	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	From now on, that’s the right thing to do, and I think that’s important.	23	2
201504	2597	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	This means we will not be in the business of telegraphing interest rate moves in the previous meeting’s statement.	23	2
201504	2598	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	I support the observations President Lockhart made on this.	23	2
201504	2599	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	An important corollary of that is that we should not be encouraging people to believe we won’t raise rates without having sent a telegraph on the issue in the previous meeting’s statement.	23	2
201504	2600	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Concerning alternative C as a stalking-horse for future statements, I don’t feel the need to include language like that in paragraphs 2 or 3 at a meeting prior to liftoff.	23	2
201504	2601	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	So I agree with Governor Tarullo—every meeting should be live, and we should, to the extent that we have the opportunity, condition people to expect that we could move without forewarning, perhaps.	23	2
201504	2602	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	A critical aspect is today’s characterization of the economy, especially in the first paragraph.	23	2

201504	2604	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	If those reports are reasonably close to what the Tealbook is forecasting, they’re going to show a rebound in consumer spending growth, and they’re going to show a firming in the monthly inflation figures.	23	2
201504	2605	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	In that case, there could well be a strong argument for raising rates in June.	23	2
201504	2606	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Now, we don’t know for sure the data will come in that way, and we don’t know for sure there won’t be some looming concerns on the horizon that alter the outlook.	23	2
201504	2607	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	We could find we’ve hit a more prolonged soft patch.	23	2
201504	2608	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	In that case, we may be inclined to delay raising rates when we get to June, and I’m open to that possibility.	23	2
201504	2609	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	But, in view of the information we’re going to get, I think it’s very important not to take June off the table.	23	2
201504	2610	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	As I said, the critical aspect is how the statement characterizes the data, and that focuses attention on the first paragraph.	23	2
201504	2611	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	The revisions that were made between the beginning of last week and the end of last week in the first paragraph were good, important, and necessary.	23	2
201504	2612	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	But I agree with President Mester that the first paragraph still has somewhat of a “glass half-empty” tone, emphasizing the empty half of the glass I guess you’d say.	23	2
201504	2613	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	I support her suggestion for changing the characterization of household spending.	23	2

201504	2615	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	I think he’s right on there.	23	2

201504	2617	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	It calls that out.	23	2

201504	2619	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	So I’d put that suggestion on the table.	23	2
201504	2620	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	Regarding the phrase “at least,”	23	2

201504	2623	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	But, on the other hand, “at least in part” means that the amount that’s attributable to transitory factors is “in part” or “greater than in part.”	23	2
201504	2624	2015-04-29	meeting	LACKER	From that point of view, “at least in part” seems to me like a more upbeat assessment than just “in part reflecting.”	23	2

201504	2629	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Bullard.	23	2
201504	2630	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2631	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m going to make three comments here.	23	2
201504	2632	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	First of all, I support President Kocherlakota’s call for a symposium on r*.	23	2
201504	2633	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We could probably benefit from getting a lot of views on the table about this important parameter in our models— how we’re thinking about it and how that thinking shapes policy choices.	23	2
201504	2634	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	So that would be an excellent thing to do, if we could fit that in at some point.	23	2
201504	2635	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	That leaves me with two comments.	23	2
201504	2636	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	One is about building credibility for data-dependent policy, and the other is about shading policy to prevent asset price bubbles.	23	2
201504	2637	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	On the credibility question, as we all discussed yesterday, recent data have been relatively weak.	23	2
201504	2638	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We have been trying to build credibility regarding a data-dependent policy being the basis for rate decisions as we go forward.	23	2
201504	2639	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I guess my assessment so far is that we have indeed built some credibility, even considerable credibility, regarding a data-dependent policy rate for the period ahead, despite the fact that we haven’t moved the policy rate for six and a half years.	23	2
201504	2640	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	And that’s an achievement.	23	2
201504	2641	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Markets—appropriately, in my view—moved the likely date of liftoff back in response to weaker-than-expected readings on the US economy during the intermeeting period.	23	2
201504	2642	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	That’s exactly what you’d expect to observe if policy is data dependent.	23	2
201504	2643	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	If we can maintain this type of credibility as the data wax and wane during 2015, we’ll be in excellent shape at the time of policy liftoff, whenever that should arrive, in the sense that there will be few surprises in the markets at that juncture.	23	2
201504	2644	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The more we can do to convince people that we’re going to react to the data and be clear about what data we’re going to react to, the better off we’ll be.	23	2
201504	2645	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m hopeful that the day of the first move will be an anticlimactic event because we have credibility that that’s what we were going to do.	23	2
201504	2646	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Then we make the move, and it’s pretty smooth.	23	2
201504	2647	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think we’re well on the way to that, but we probably have more work to do during the spring.	23	2
201504	2648	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Let me turn to the second comment on the shading of policy.	23	2
201504	2649	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The staff’s forecast is that there will be a smooth glide to the steady state.	23	2
201504	2650	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This forecast envisions unemployment only a few ticks lower at the end of the forecast horizon in 2017 than it is today.	23	2
201504	2651	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is not my baseline forecast.	23	2
201504	2652	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I envision unemployment falling well into the 4 percent range over the forecast horizon, barring any large negative shock to the US economy.	23	2
201504	2653	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is what happened in the 1990s and in the 2000s, and I think it’s a good baseline forecast of what’s going to happen again this time.	23	2
201504	2654	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Simultaneously, the labor market conditions index, which takes into account all aspects of labor market performance, will continue to rise far above its average value.	23	2
201504	2655	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	So we’ll be talking about, in the years ahead, a very robust labor market performance when compared with metrics on past labor market performance in the United States.	23	2
201504	2656	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is going to constitute a boom phase for the US economy, notwithstanding this morning’s GDP report, which I think is going to prove to be temporary.	23	2
201504	2657	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The boom will be associated with about 3 percent growth, at least for a time, in the US economy.	23	2
201504	2658	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	That 3 percent growth, while not stellar, is still about 1 percent higher than the potential growth rate for the US economy.	23	2
201504	2659	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is very similar to the 1990s, when we had years in which we were at 4 percent in an economy that had a 3 percent potential growth rate.	23	2
201504	2660	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’ll be growing at 3 percent for a time in an economy that has a 2 percent potential growth rate.	23	2
201504	2661	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Inflation will rise, go through the inflation target, and be higher than 2 percent over parts of the forecast horizon.	23	2
201504	2662	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	During this period, interest rates will remain exceptionally low by historical standards.	23	2
201504	2663	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’ve already committed ourselves to that by saying that, even when we start to normalize, it would be very small and very gradual.	23	2
201504	2664	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	You could throw in that my view is that this policy rate, as we’re envisioning it over the next couple of years, will be below the likely natural rate of interest, the r* value.	23	2
201504	2665	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	In this sense, we’re going to provide accommodation as currently envisioned all of the way through this boom phase for the US economy.	23	2
201504	2666	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is how I’m thinking about it.	23	2
201504	2667	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Now, the boom period, combined with exceptionally low policy rate settings, is a recipe for asset price bubbles.	23	2
201504	2668	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Asset price bubbles have plagued the US economy during the 1990s and 2000s and have been a major point of debate at this Committee over the past 20 years.	23	2
201504	2669	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	These bubbles are not in our models.	23	2
201504	2670	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	They were not in the past 20 years, they’re not in our models today, and we don’t see any component of this in our standard presentations on what we should do about monetary policy.	23	2
201504	2671	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	So we do not have a good understanding of where these bubbles come from or how to control them when they occur.	23	2
201504	2672	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Furthermore, bursting asset price bubbles can have devastating consequences for the United States and the global economy.	23	2
201504	2673	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	It’s no small matter if this thing gets going and develops.	23	2
201504	2674	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The basic strategy, in my view, should be to head off this kind of possibility.	23	2
201504	2675	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’ve had boom-and-bust cycles during the 1990s and the 2000s.	23	2
201504	2676	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	What you’d like to do this time around is not to have that boom-and-bust cycle.	23	2
201504	2677	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Then you’ll get a longer expansion, and you’ll get better outcomes for everybody.	23	2
201504	2678	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	It’s true that we have an improved macroprudential stance today, and that will help mitigate some of the more severe consequences of a bursting bubble.	23	2
201504	2679	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	But untested macroprudential tools alone are, in my view, insufficient to rely on, given the exceptionally dire consequences of bursting asset bubbles not just for the US economy, but also for the global economy.	23	2
201504	2680	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The prudent policy, in my view, is to combine our efforts on macroprudential policy with a sensible monetary policy that hedges our bets in the direction of prevention of the kind of asset price boom-and-bust cycles that we’ve experienced during the 1990s and 2000s.	23	2
201504	2681	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think we should be shading in the direction of slightly higher interest rates than we would otherwise have, on the grounds that an ounce of prevention is worth a pound of cure on this dimension.	23	2
201504	2682	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We don’t have very good knowledge of what these bubbles are or how they form, so we should be hedging our bets on this.	23	2
201504	2683	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is an important reason to get going on a modest normalization program when the opportunity arrives, which I expect it will later this year.	23	2
201504	2684	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Let me turn just to alternative B for a minute.	23	2
201504	2685	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I support alternative B, as written, for today, without the “at least” phrase in the first paragraph.	23	2
201504	2686	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The “in part” covers the bases there, and I don’t think we need to add anything more to that.	23	2
201504	2687	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I do agree with President Mester’s suggested change.	23	2
201504	2688	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	The wording “Growth in household spending declined; households’ real incomes rose strongly” is a little bit jarring, and I like President Mester’s suggestion there that would smooth that out a little bit.	23	2
201504	2689	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	I agree with Governor Tarullo and President Lacker that we should not try to reintroduce calendar-dependent language as liftoff nears.	23	2
201504	2690	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’re trying to get away from that, so we should not try to do that.	23	2
201504	2691	2015-04-29	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2692	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	23	2
201504	2693	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Evans.	23	2

201504	2696	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I continue to believe that economic conditions will most likely be consistent with an appropriate time for liftoff being sometime in 2016.	23	2

201504	2704	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I agree completely that we have moved to the point at which it’s a meeting-by-meeting decision.	23	2
201504	2705	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	They’re all live decisions on the rate increase, so data conditionality, by itself, is all we need.	23	2
201504	2706	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I don’t think we need special language, either, to signal our intent.	23	2
201504	2707	2015-04-29	meeting	EVANS	I agree completely with Governor Tarullo on the paragraph 1 language on inflation.	23	2

201504	2726	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2727	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	I support alternative B and the latitude it offers the Committee to consider a change in policy at its next meeting.	23	2
201504	2728	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	In terms of guidance that we can glean from policy rules, the benchmarks in the Tealbook, Book B, continue to generally suggest that the funds rate should be higher than its current setting.	23	2
201504	2729	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Granted, the equilibrium real rate, or r*, we’ve been discussing remains depressed and raises questions as to whether these benchmarks are providing appropriate guidance today.	23	2
201504	2730	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	At the same time, common frameworks used to estimate r* also provide an estimate of potential GDP in which declines in r* go hand in hand with lower potential.	23	2
201504	2731	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	For example, the current Laubach-Williams framework indicates that the equilibrium real rate is negative, though it also implies that output is actually above potential.	23	2
201504	2732	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	So, while policy should incorporate a lower equilibrium real rate into the appropriate setting of the funds rate, it also should not neglect the implications for potential GDP and the output gap.	23	2
201504	2733	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	For example, using the lower r* measure and positive output gap in the Taylor (1999) rule with inertia suggests a higher current funds rate.	23	2
201504	2734	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	The Taylor (1993) rule indicates a setting above 1 percent, even with the lower equilibrium real rate.	23	2
201504	2735	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	I don’t take the positive output gap to completely reflect current conditions by any means, since some labor market slack remains.	23	2
201504	2736	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	However, this example illustrates that taking on board a lower equilibrium real rate may also imply a smaller output gap.	23	2
201504	2737	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Finally, although this is not a decision for today, I note increasing uncertainty about the timing and effects of ceasing reinvestments.	23	2
201504	2738	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	I’m a bit concerned in looking at the market participant surveys and noting that expectations on the timing of when reinvestments will be ended are quite diffuse.	23	2
201504	2739	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	For example, a slight majority of dealers expect Treasury reinvestments to end in the first quarter of 2016, but then expectations are spread over the next four quarters into 2017.	23	2
201504	2740	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	This is somewhat concerning, especially given the risk highlighted in the QS report and recent IMF financial stability reports that longer-term rates could increase sharply approaching liftoff.	23	2
201504	2741	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Or the Committee could be facing another conundrum in which longer-term rates remain low.	23	2
201504	2742	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	I think we should try to manage some of this risk by providing more guidance on the timing of reinvestment, perhaps incorporating it into the SEP or perhaps just distributing it on an internal basis for our own use, to think about expectations on when we anticipate ceasing reinvestments.	23	2
201504	2743	2015-04-29	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you.	23	2

201504	2823	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I would like to second the comments of President Kocherlakota and President Bullard about having more of a discussion regarding the natural rate of interest, with r* estimates obviously being at the center of this.	23	2
201504	2824	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I get a lot of feedback, pro and con, about this, and there is some literature developing on this besides just the Laubach-Williams model.	23	2

201504	2826	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The basic logic of Laubach-Williams is actually pretty simple.	23	2
201504	2827	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Over the past five years, output growth has been averaging 2.3 percent—I’m picking up on President Kocherlakota’s point yesterday about how Laubach-Williams works.	23	2
201504	2828	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The trend growth in our model is 2.0 percent.	23	2
201504	2829	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think that’s consistent with most people’s views.	23	2
201504	2830	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	So we’re getting growth of 0.3 percentage point above trend over five years, while the real federal funds rate has been about minus 1.7.	23	2
201504	2831	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The only way you can interpret this statistically is to think that the real rate gap— basically, the amount of monetary stimulus—is very modest over the past five years, and this hasn’t changed in our model.	23	2
201504	2832	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	What Laubach-Williams is telling you is simple.	23	2
201504	2833	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We’ve had negative real rates for five straight years.	23	2
201504	2834	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Output growth has been just a smidgen above potential.	23	2
201504	2835	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Therefore, there’s really not much monetary stimulus relative to the neutral rate going on.	23	2
201504	2836	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This is something we should discuss further, but, basically, that’s how the model interprets the data.	23	2
201504	2837	2015-04-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	If the equilibrium real rate really were 2, growth would have been a lot faster than 2.3.	23	2

201504	2852	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2853	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I’d like to first discuss briefly the inflation target issue that arose yesterday, and then turn to the monetary policy decision and explain why I support alternative B.	23	2
201504	2854	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Yesterday President Rosengren, supported by President Kocherlakota and one or two others, suggested raising the target inflation rate, a suggestion that’s also been made by Olivier Blanchard.	23	2
201504	2855	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I believe that we should constantly be reexamining all of our assumptions about monetary policy and the behavior of the economy, but that, particularly in the case of targets, we need to be very careful about our public stance.	23	2
201504	2856	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	With regard to our public stance, we fixed a target inflation rate in 2011 and announced a 2 percent target in 2012.	23	2
201504	2857	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	The setting of a target inflation rate was a major achievement and, to those outside the System, a major surprise.	23	2
201504	2858	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	We need to be very careful indeed about reopening that decision and that discussion.	23	2
201504	2859	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	The argument for raising the target inflation rate is that the higher target would permit normal monetary policy to attain a more negative real rate of interest than is now possible.	23	2
201504	2860	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	That is true, but there’s not so far been public discussion of the negative aspects of a higher inflation target.	23	2
201504	2861	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	The main cost, I believe, relates to Alan Greenspan’s definition of the desired inflation rate as a rate such that inflation is not taken into account in routine economic decisions.	23	2
201504	2862	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	In particular, at around 4 percent inflation, we very likely would see the return of indexation into relatively short-term contracts, including labor contracts.	23	2
201504	2863	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Monetary policy becomes much more difficult in heavily indexed economies, in part because the inflation rate typically becomes more variable the higher the indexation rate and because the probability that the dynamics of inflation become unstable increases.	23	2
201504	2864	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	A second cost relates to the credibility of the inflation target and of the FOMC.	23	2
201504	2865	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	It is possible that we should, at some future point, raise the inflation target.	23	2
201504	2866	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	But if we go out now and start a public discussion about the inflation target, we will undoubtedly be asked why we’re raising the target and what the effects of doing so are likely to be.	23	2
201504	2867	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	There are answers to these questions, but the answers may well reduce our credibility precisely at a time when reasonable people regard us as the most credible and most professional of the macro policymaking institutions in the United States.	23	2
201504	2868	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	We—that is, the participants in this meeting—should not take that risk.	23	2
201504	2869	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	We should not go public with any doubts that we might have about the goals of monetary policy.	23	2
201504	2870	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	At the moment, we’re in the middle of the process of trying to normalize monetary policy.	23	2
201504	2871	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	We should always be reconsidering our policy framework, but there are times to go public and times not to do so.	23	2
201504	2872	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	That is why our discussions of the target inflation rate should continue within the Federal Reserve System and within the Committee, and that is why it is fine that Eric raised the issue in yesterday’s go-round, but we should not go public with that discussion.	23	2
201504	2873	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Turning now to the monetary policy decision, yesterday’s discussion, in which almost every participant said, rightly, that the key issue is whether first-quarter weakness is transitory or a signal of a longer-term weakening of growth, and this morning’s announcement of the first estimate of first-quarter GDP growth make it clear that we should wait before making a decision to change the interest rate.	23	2
201504	2874	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I say that while bearing in mind an almost true maxim I once heard: “The situation is never clearer in the future.	23	2
201504	2875	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	It’s just unclear in a different way.”	23	2
201504	2876	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	[Laughter]	23	2
201504	2877	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I support alternative B, in which the first three paragraphs do a good job of showing that we are watching the data carefully, and that the probability of a June liftoff has declined, but that June remains on the table.	23	2
201504	2878	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	As weak as the incoming data have been and as threatening as the many black clouds that are out there are, it remains important to keep June as a possible liftoff date.	23	2
201504	2879	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	How could it be possible?	23	2
201504	2880	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Well, we’ll receive two more employment reports before the June meeting.	23	2
201504	2881	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	If we see very high payroll gains in April and May and if the unemployment rate comes down to, say, 5.3 percent while the spending data firm as expected, it would be reasonable to argue for liftoff.	23	2
201504	2882	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Some might suggest that we should wait just a little longer to be sure, since September isn’t really that different from June, to perfect our communications about liftoff or to reexamine the tools we intend to use to raise the interest rate.	23	2
201504	2883	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	That would mean that even in the face of very good data, the June versus September decision would be a close call.	23	2
201504	2884	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	But in making that call, we need also to take into account the importance of our credibility, which would require us to act when the conditions for acting that we have set out are fulfilled.	23	2
201504	2885	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Turning to the bigger picture, my views on when to lift off were influenced by the very helpful paper that was sent to this Committee in January by Oliver de Groot, Etienne Gagnon, and Robert Tetlow, whose results I quoted in the March FOMC meeting.	23	2
201504	2886	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	In that paper, they compared the probabilities that policymakers might regret their decisions under early and late liftoff scenarios.	23	2
201504	2887	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Using random draws from historical shocks, they show that the probability that we would fall behind the curve and wish that we had tightened sooner was negligible if we go on the early side but was close to 10 percent if we wait a year longer.	23	2
201504	2888	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	In contrast, the difference between the probabilities that we would need to retrace our steps if we go earlier, 10 percent, rather than later, 5 percent, was not as great.	23	2
201504	2889	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Now, it’s a very good paper, but it’s not obvious where we are today relative to going early or going late.	23	2
201504	2890	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Nonetheless, all in all, I found that analysis very useful, and it helped persuade me that we should seriously be contemplating lifting off soon.	23	2
201504	2891	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	It further helped convince me that “early and gradual” is a much better approach to raising the interest rate than “late and steep.”	23	2
201504	2892	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Of course, in the event, we may have to raise the rate faster or slower than we anticipate, but, at this stage, we have to work with expectations.	23	2
201504	2893	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I’d also like to relate to the argument that we’re only in this relatively good position on employment and this potentially good position on inflation because the interest rate is so low, and that, therefore, we should not move anytime soon.	23	2
201504	2894	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	It’s undoubtedly true that the US economy would not be in this relatively favorable position if the FOMC had not moved rapidly after Lehman Brothers to the zero lower bound and then undertaken three rounds of QE as well as Operation Twist.	23	2
201504	2895	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I believe history will show that the Federal Reserve—together with the decision to recapitalize the banking system, in which the Treasury and the Congress played critical roles—saved the United States from a second Great Depression.	23	2
201504	2896	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	But we need to realize that, at some point, we have to put the interest rate back into action as a tool of monetary policy.	23	2
201504	2897	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	In so doing, we’ll not only begin to activate a more normal way of undertaking monetary policy, but we’ll also be sending an extremely important signal to businesses and households that, after seven years, the American economy is ready to exit the Great Recession and begin the return to normal.	23	2
201504	2898	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	When should we do this?	23	2
201504	2899	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	The answer is, we should do it when an interest rate higher than we have now is still consistent with the closing of the output gap.	23	2
201504	2900	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	I believe that, on the inflation rate, the staff forecasts we heard yesterday from Bill Wascher are far less pessimistic than what I’ve heard around this table this morning.	23	2
201504	2901	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	It’s true we’ve got negative inflation in the first quarter, but it’s very important to note that forecasts of core PCE inflation are somewhere well above 1 percent, and that when you take the special factors into account, it’s expected to rise not to 0.7 percent, but to 1.7 percent, which is very close to target.	23	2
201504	2902	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	We should also note that even the r* in the Tealbook, Book B, is about 110 basis points above our current real federal funds rate, suggesting that a sooner liftoff would not be incompatible with further progress toward our goals if the situation improves.	23	2
201504	2903	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Or, to put the criterion in the words of alternative B, we should move when the Committee “has seen further improvement in the labor market and is reasonably confident that inflation will move back to its 2 percent objective over the medium term.”	23	2
201504	2904	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	And I expect that will happen reasonably soon.	23	2
201504	2905	2015-04-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	23	2
201504	2906	2015-04-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	23	2

201504	2911	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	More broadly, paragraph 1 initially struck me, and continues to strike me, as fairly downbeat.	23	2
201504	2912	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	But what saves it for me is this comment about how household real income is rising strongly, which I think really sets out our narrative for just how much we’re counting on consumer spending and is a pretty strong statement in a context of weak spending.	23	2
201504	2913	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	So, overall, it achieves a level of balance, and I’m fine with it with those changes.	23	2
201504	2914	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	In terms of forward guidance, I’ll just add that I believe it’s a very bad idea to surprise the market with liftoff.	23	2
201504	2915	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I would avoid calendar-based guidance.	23	2
201504	2916	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I would let the data speak.	23	2
201504	2917	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I would let the statement, the minutes, and the speeches speak.	23	2
201504	2918	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I do think the market will get it.	23	2
201504	2919	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	It’s very unlikely that the people in this room would be approaching a decision to lift off the interest rate, with all of the attention we’re getting, and that this would be a surprise to the market.	23	2
201504	2920	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	That’s incredibly unlikely.	23	2
201504	2921	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	In fact, my guess is, as we approach that, it’s not going to be a surprise to the market, in light of all of the transparency and all of the attention being paid.	23	2
201504	2922	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	More broadly, I think that policy is right where it’s supposed to be—looking at incoming data to see whether the liftoff narrative still holds together, that narrative being reasonable growth that is strong enough to support further improvement in the labor market at a meaningful pace and inflation moving up to 2 percent in the medium term.	23	2
201504	2923	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	The sense I had at the beginning of the year was that the labor market would continue to heal fairly quickly in a context of moderate growth, and I think there’s a better-than-even chance that something like that narrative will reemerge as we leave the first quarter behind, just as it did in 2014.	23	2
201504	2924	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	So, as I said yesterday, I feel good about where the current Tealbook forecast is, and I consider that to be still on the same path we were on—a little bit slower, but a reasonable path.	23	2
201504	2925	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I do have to admit I have a concern now, though, that the speed limit on US growth may now be closer to 2 percent than 3 percent, because of the drag generated by the behavior of net exports, and that improvement in the labor market will also slow down as a result.	23	2
201504	2926	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	Time—and data—will tell.	23	2
201504	2927	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	Monetary policy can respond and really has already done so through expectations.	23	2
201504	2928	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	It may limit further tightening but doesn’t feel as though it can reverse the tightening in financial conditions that we have already experienced.	23	2
201504	2929	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	In the case of returning closer to the old path that had strong job growth, we’re close to the natural rate.	23	2
201504	2930	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	Monetary policy works with long lags.	23	2
201504	2931	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	Zero isn’t the right number.	23	2
201504	2932	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	This remains for me a very satisfying approach if we do get back to that path, and, again, that’s my modal case.	23	2
201504	2933	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	The lower path is just much tougher.	23	2
201504	2934	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	We’re still close to the natural rate.	23	2
201504	2935	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	We’re still eliminating slack, but at a much slower pace, and I think that, more important, the economy is more vulnerable to an unwanted tightening in financial conditions and probably in a nonlinear way.	23	2
201504	2936	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I would still want to lift off, just a little bit later.	23	2
201504	2937	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	But I would point out that the communication challenges and the risks are much less attractive.	23	2
201504	2938	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	I’d be very happy to see data come in that would justify liftoff in June or at any meeting subsequent to that.	23	2
201504	2939	2015-04-29	meeting	POWELL	But, having resisted predictions so far for the most part, I’ll continue to do so today.	23	2

201504	2953	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	For those who worry that paragraph 1 is too downbeat, I would note this is significantly counterbalanced by the fact that alternative B makes few changes to paragraphs 2 and 3 of the March statement, including importantly reiterating that the risks to the outlook and the labor market are nearly balanced rather than emphasizing downside risks in light of recent data.	23	2
201504	2954	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In my view, the incoming data, on balance, do not suggest we’re appreciably closer to our two conditions for liftoff.	23	2

201504	2957	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	My reading of the data on unemployment, labor force participation, and the number of employees working part time for economic reasons is that slack remains.	23	2
201504	2958	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Nonetheless, we’ll have two additional labor market readings to help with our assessment before the June FOMC deliberations.	23	2
201504	2959	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Meanwhile, the modest improvement we’ve seen in putting a floor under inflation falls short of our standard of reasonable confidence.	23	2
201504	2960	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	We’ve seen some encouraging movement in core CPI, although core PCE remains persistently soft relative to our 2 percent target.	23	2
201504	2961	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	The recent firming of oil prices and the recent plateauing in dollar appreciation raise the possibility that we might be seeing an inflection point, but further appreciation seems likely, accelerating wage growth has yet to materialize, and market-based measures of inflation expectations remain soft, although they’ve shown some improvement as the price of oil has firmed.	23	2
201504	2962	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	It’s possible that the data we will receive in the coming months will reveal the weakness in the data to have been a temporary aberration, and that momentum in underlying activity is undiminished.	23	2
201504	2963	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	It could also be that incoming data and economic developments abroad will show foreign activity to be on more stable footing than now appears to be the case.	23	2
201504	2964	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	If so and if price inflation and measures of inflation expectations show further signs of firming, then liftoff could be appropriate.	23	2
201504	2965	2015-04-29	meeting	BRAINARD	On balance, my assessment is that, although June should remain on the table, the probability of liftoff has shifted from June to later in the year.	23	2
